Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
6 min
First Type of Fasād al-I'tibār: Narrow vs Broad
Defines the first type of invalid analogy due to scope mismatch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an analogy between two rulings is invalid due to scope mismatch (tadyīq/tawsī'a) and formulate the correct response (jawāb) to refute such an objection.

Core Idea
An analogy fails if one ruling is restricted and the other expanded; the refutation is to show their causes are equal in the specific case.

In jadal, one common way to attack an analogy is to claim that the two rulings being compared operate on different scopes—one narrow, one broad. This lesson introduces the first type of fasād al-i'tibār, where such a scope mismatch is alleged. You will learn to analyze these objections and construct a valid response by demonstrating that in the specific locus of the cause, the rulings are equivalent in strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings being compared
Find the two rulings (aḥkām) that the proponent of the analogy is trying to analogize. For example, in the passage: the beginning of marriage (ibtidā' al-nikāh) and its continuity (istidāma) during iḥrām.
2
Determine the scope of each ruling
Check whether each ruling is based on restriction (tadyīq) or expansion (tawsī'a). The objector claims one is narrow and the other broad — e.g., continuity is stronger (aqwā) than the beginning.
3
Identify the objector's challenge
The objector (mu'tariḍ) argues that because the scopes differ, the analogy is invalid: 'one cannot consider one by the other.' This is the first type of fasād al-i'tibār.
4
Formulate the response (jawāb)
The proponent (mudda'i) responds by showing that in the specific place where the cause ('illa) is applied, both rulings are equal in strength. For example, in the 'illa for iḥrām, the beginning and continuity are equally valid bases.
The jawāb does not deny the general difference in strength but localizes the parity.
5
Apply to other examples
Practice the pattern: e.g., slavery vs. emancipation, or emancipation vs. sale — the objector says one is weak, the other strong; the jawāb shows equality in the specific cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن يعتبر حكم بحكم وأحدهما مبناه على التضييق والآخر مبناه على التوسعة فلا أحدهما بالآخر، أو يعتبر الابتداء بالاستدانة كاعتبار ابتداء النكاح باستدامته في الإحرام، فيقال: الاستدامة أقوى والابتداء أضعف، فلا يعتبر أحدهما بالآخر، أو يعتبر الرق بالعتق أو العتق بالبيع ومبنى أحدهما على الضعف ومبنى الآخر على القوة، فلا يجوز اعتبار أحدهما بالآخر، والجواب أن يبين أنهما في الموضع الذي علل سواء
First: that one considers a ruling by a ruling when one of them is based on restriction and the other on expansion, so neither is analogous to the other; or one considers the beginning by borrowing like considering the beginning of marriage by its continuity in ihram, so it is said: continuity is stronger and the beginning is weaker, so one cannot consider the one by the other; or one considers slavery by emancipation or emancipation by sale, and the basis of one is weakness and the basis of the other is strength, so it is not permissible to consider one by the other; and the answer is that one should explain that in the place of causation they are equal.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage explains the first type of invalid consideration (fasād al-i'tibār) in jadal. The argument is that when a ruling is based on restriction (tadyīq) and another on expansion (tawsī'a), they cannot be analogized. Three examples are given: (1) comparing the beginning of an act (like borrowing) with its continuity (like marriage in ihram) where continuity is stronger; (2) comparing slavery to emancipation, where one is weak and the other strong; (3) comparing emancipation to sale, similarly unequal. The response (jawāb) to such an objection is to show that in the specific place where the cause is applied, both rulings are actually equal in strength. Thus, the invalidity is refuted by demonstrating the parity of the cause.

Key Terms
اعتبار
تضييق
توسعة
الاستدامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first type of fasād al-i'tibār, why is it invalid to consider the beginning of marriage (ibtidā' al-nikāh) based on its continuity (istidāma) in iḥrām?
A) Because the beginning is stronger than continuity.
B) Because continuity is stronger than the beginning, so they differ in strength.
C) Because both are equal in strength.
D) Because iḥrām invalidates any analogy.

Key Takeaway
The first type of invalid analogy occurs when one ruling is based on restriction and the other on expansion; the refutation is to prove their causes are equal in the specific context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify another fiqh example (e.g., comparing zakāt on livestock vs. trade goods) where one ruling might be considered restricted and the other expanded. Explain why a mu'tariḍ might claim the analogy is invalid and construct the correct jawāb showing equality in the cause.
?
In a hypothetical jadal session, one student proposes an analogy between the prohibition of wine and the prohibition of nabīdh. Another objects that wine is restricted (tadyīq) and nabīdh is expanded (tawsī'a). Formulate a response that refutes this objection.

Defending Analogy Against the Broader Ruling Objection
Explains the second type of invalid analogy using the example of kaffāra and qadā'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a valid response to the fallacy of considering a ruling by a broader ruling in jadal.

Core Idea
A broader ruling does not invalidate analogy when the cause is identical in the specific case.

In this passage, we see a classic debate between Maliki and Shafi'i jurists over whether expiation (kaffāra) for eating in Ramadan can be analogized to making up missed fasts (qadā'). The Shafi'i objects that qadā' is broader because it applies to vows, while kaffāra does not. The Maliki's response shows how to defend an analogy when the cause ('illa) is shared in the specific instance. This lesson will teach you how to detect and respond to the 'broader ruling' objection in dialectical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original analogy
The Maliki claims that kaffāra is analogous to qadā' because both are rulings that apply to violators of the sanctity of Ramadan.
The analogy is based on a common cause (violating Ramadan's sanctity).
2
Spot the Shafi'i's objection
The Shafi'i points out that qadā' is broader (أوسع) than kaffāra, as qadā' applies to cases like vows (نذر) where kaffāra does not, so the analogy is invalid.
The objection is that the broader scope of one ruling breaks the analogy.
3
Analyze the Maliki's response
The Maliki responds that in the specific issue of Ramadan, the cause is identical for both rulings: both kaffāra and qadā' are triggered by violating the fast through intercourse or eating. The broader scope of qadā' in other contexts (like vows) is irrelevant because the cause is present equally here.
The response restricts the analogy to the case where the cause is shared.
4
Apply the principle to similar debates
When defending an analogy against an objection of broader scope, you must show that the cause (ʿilla) is present in the contested case, regardless of other applications of the broader ruling.
This is a key tactic in jadal (dialectical argumentation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن يعتبر حكما بحكم وأحدهما أوسع، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على وجوب الكفارة في رمضان بأن هذا حكم يلزم المنتهك لحرمة رمضان بالجماع، فلزم المنتهك لحرمته بالأكل كالقضاء، فيقول الشافعي: هذا اعتبار فاسد، لأنك اعتبرت الكفارة بالقضاء والقضاء أوسع من الكفارة، ألا ترى يجب في موضع النذر ولا تجب الكفارة، فلا يصح اعتبار أحدهما بالآخر، والجواب أن يبين أن في الموضع الذي علل هما سواء، وذلك أن النذر لا صوم مدخل للكفارة فيه وللقضاء فيه مدخل وأما في هذه المسألة فإن صوم رمضان للقضاء وللكفارة فيه مدخل ألا ترى أنه إذا جامع وجب على كل واحد منهما، فلما تعلق بالأكل عمدًا أحدهما، أن يتعلق به الآخر
Second: that one considers a ruling by a ruling when one of them is broader, such as when the Maliki argues for the obligation of expiation (kaffāra) in Ramadan by saying that this is a ruling that applies to the violator of the sanctity of Ramadan by intercourse, so it applies to the violator of its sanctity by eating, like qadā'; then the Shafi'i says: this consideration is invalid, because you have considered kaffāra by qadā', and qadā' is broader than kaffāra, for qadā' is obligatory in the case of a vow (nadhr) while kaffāra is not, so it is not valid to consider one by the other; and the answer is that one should explain that in the place of causation they are equal, and that is because a vow does not enter into kaffāra but enters into qadā', while in this issue, Ramadan fasting enters into both qadā' and kaffāra, because if one has intercourse (during Ramadan), it becomes necessary on each one of them, so when it is attached to eating deliberately, the other is also attached.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of invalid consideration (fasād al-i'tibār) in dialectical argumentation. The example is from a debate between Maliki and Shafi'i jurists. The Maliki argues that kaffāra (expiation for eating in Ramadan) is analogous to qadā' (making up missed fasts) because both are caused by violating the sanctity of Ramadan. The Shafi'i objects on the grounds that qadā' is broader than kaffāra (qadā' applies to vows, kaffāra does not), so the analogy fails. The response (jawāb) is that in the specific context of Ramadan, both rulings share the same cause (al-'illa). The Maliki can argue that qadā' and kaffāra both apply to the violator of Ramadan's sanctity, regardless of whether the action was intercourse or eating. The point is that the broader ruling's extra cases (like vows) are irrelevant when the cause is present. Thus, the analogy stands when the cause is identical.

Key Terms
كفارة
قضاء
نذر
اعتبار فاسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second type of fasād al-i'tibār, the Shafi'i objects to the analogy between kaffāra and qadā' because:
A) Kaffāra is broader than qadā'.
B) Qadā' is broader than kaffāra.
C) Both are equal in scope.
D) The cause is different.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the objection that a ruling is broader does not automatically invalidate an analogy; the defender can show that the cause is identical in the specific case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider another debate: Some jurists analogize the prohibition of selling dates for dates in excess (ribā) to the prohibition of selling gold for gold in excess. An objector says the ruling on gold is broader because it applies also to silver. Construct a response following the Maliki's strategy, showing that the cause (ribā al-faḍl) is present in both, regardless of gold's extra applications.

Identifying the Third Type of Invalid Analogy: Differing in Analogous Rulings
Explains the third type of invalid analogy with a juristic example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases where a branch and origin differ in analogous rulings and construct a valid response by distinguishing the specific ruling.

Core Idea
An analogy is invalid if the two cases differ in the very rulings that are claimed to be analogous.

The third type of fasād al-i'tibār occurs when someone analogizes a branch to an origin, but they differ in the very analogies (nazā'ir) that are being compared. This lesson uses the debate between Hanafi and Maliki jurists over qisas between a Muslim and a dhimmi to illustrate how to detect and refute this error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed analogy
Find the branch (far') and origin (asl) in the given example: the Hanafi compares a Muslim (far') to a dhimmi (asl) for qisas, based on the common element of protected blood.
2
Spot the difference in analogous rulings
What are the nazā'ir where the two differ? The Maliki points to the hadd for slander (qadhf) and rights to illustrate that Muslim and dhimmi are treated differently in many rulings.
These differences are used as grounds for objecting to the analogy.
3
Determine the specific ruling under dispute
The ruling in question is killing (qatl) in retaliation (qisas), not the other rulings mentioned. The Hanafi argues that killing has its own distinct basis.
4
Analyze the response: distinguish the specific ruling
The Hanafi shows that the ruling on killing differs from hadd and qisas in their conditions. For example, hadd for qadhf depends on chastity, but qisas does not; and qisas is not exacted by a non-Muslim from a Muslim even if he had a right, unlike the rule for killing.
The response demonstrates that the difference in other rulings does not necessarily invalidate the analogy for this specific case.
5
Apply the principle
State the rule: if the two differ in the analogies, the analogy is invalid unless the specific ruling has independent grounds that override the differences. The Hanafi successfully shows this.
This is the correct method to defend or reject an analogy in jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن يعتبر فرعًا بأصل وهما مختلفان في نظائر الحكم، كاعتبار الصغير بالكبير في الصلاة والصوم والحج، وكاعتبار المرأة بالرجل في القتل بالردة وهما مختلفان في القتل بالكفر الأصلي، وما أشبه ذلك، والجواب أن يبين أن ما ذكر أنه نظير للحكم ليس بنظير له وإنما نظير الحكم غيره، وهما متفقان فيه من ذلك مثلا، اعتبار المسلم بالكافر، فيستدل الحنفي على قتل المسلم بالذمي قصاصا، بأن الذمي محقون الدم على التأبيد، فوجب أن يقتل به المسلم كالمسلم؛ فيقول المالكي: اعتبرت الكافر بالمسلم، وذلك لا يجوز لأن الأصول قد فرقت بينهما، لأن المسلم أتم حرمة من الكافر ألا ترى أنه لا يحد بقذفه ويحد بقذف المسلم، ولا يثبت للكافر على المسلم حق ويثبت للمسلم، والجواب أن يبين أن حكم القتل مخالف للحد والاقتصاص، ألا ترى أن الحد يجب بالعفة والصلاح ولا يجب للمسلم الفاسق، بخلاف القتل فإنه يجب للمسلم الفاسق على المسلم العفيف، وأما الاقتصاص فلا يجوز أن يستوفيه الكافر من المسلم وإن ثبت له عليه حال الكفر، بخلاف القتل فإنه يستوفى للكافر من المسلم حال الإسلام إذا ثبت له عليه حال الكفر، فافترقا
Third: that one considers a branch (far') by an origin (aṣl) while they differ in analogous rulings, such as considering a minor by an adult in prayer, fasting, and pilgrimage, and considering a woman by a man in killing for apostasy while they differ in killing by original unbelief, and the like; and the answer is that one should explain that what is mentioned as an analog for the ruling is not its analog, rather the analog of the ruling is something else in which they agree; for example, considering a Muslim by a non-Muslim, so the Hanafi argues for killing a Muslim for a dhimmi in retaliation (qisas), because the dhimmi's blood is protected perpetually, so it is necessary to kill the Muslim for him as one kills a Muslim for a Muslim; then the Maliki says: you have considered the non-Muslim by the Muslim, and that is not permissible because the principles have differentiated between them, because a Muslim is more inviolable than a non-Muslim, for you see that the punishment for slander (qadhf) is not inflicted for slandering a non-Muslim but is inflicted for slandering a Muslim, and no right is established for a non-Muslim against a Muslim while it is established for a Muslim; and the answer is that one should explain that the ruling of killing is different from the hadd (fixed punishment) and retaliation (qisas), for you see that the hadd is obligatory based on chastity and righteousness and is not obligatory for a sinful Muslim, unlike killing which is obligatory for a sinful Muslim against a chaste Muslim; and as for qisas, it is not permissible for a non-Muslim to exact it from a Muslim even if he had a right against him in a state of disbelief, unlike killing, which is exacted for a non-Muslim from a Muslim in a state of Islam if he had a right against him in a state of disbelief; so they are different.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage explains the third type of invalid consideration (fasād al-i'tibār). It occurs when one tries to analogize a branch (far') to an origin (aṣl) while they differ in analogous rulings (nazā'ir). The generic examples include minor/adult and woman/man in acts of worship, and a specific example involves the Hanafi view on qisas between a Muslim and a dhimmi. The Hanafi argues that a Muslim can be killed in retaliation for a dhimmi because the dhimmi's blood is protected (maḥqūn al-dam). The Maliki objects that the principles of law differentiate between Muslim and non-Muslim in many rulings (e.g., the hadd for slander, rights). The Hanafi's answer is that the ruling on killing is distinct from those other rulings because killing is based on the perpetuity of protection, not on personal status like chastity. Therefore, the analogy is valid because the cause for killing is common between Muslim and dhimmi in this specific case.

Key Terms
فرع
أصل
نظير
قصاص
ذمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the third type, the Hanafi argues that a Muslim can be killed in retaliation for a dhimni because:
A) The dhimni's blood is not protected.
B) The dhimni's blood is protected perpetually.
C) Muslims are less protected than dhimmis.
D) The hadd for slander is the same.

Key Takeaway
When a branch and origin differ in many analogous rulings, the analogy may still be valid if the specific ruling has its own independent grounds that override those differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the example: 'A minor is compared to an adult for the obligation of prayer.' Identify whether this is the third type of invalid analogy. Explain why or why not, using the criteria from the passage.
?
In a debate, the opponent claims that woman and man differ in rulings on inheritance and testimony, so they cannot be analogized in the ruling on apostasy killing. How would you respond following the Hanafi response pattern?

Al-Tūfī's Rule on Fasād al-I'tibār
States al-Tūfī's principle that fasād al-i'tibār renders the entire argument invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why fasād al-i'tibār invalidates the entire argument according to al-Tūfī and identify the role of the mustadill's proof.

Core Idea
If the proof is defective, the argument falls — al-Tūfī's principle of total invalidation.

This lesson focuses on a key dialectical principle from Imam al-Tūfī: when an arguer's proof is shown to be invalid or contradictory to stronger principles, the entire argument collapses. This is not a partial flaw but a total ruin, because the ruling (ḥukm) depends entirely on the soundness of the proof (dalīl). Understanding this rule is crucial for evaluating arguments in jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Learn the terms 'fasād al-i'tibār' (invalidity of consideration) and 'mustadill' (the arguer). They are central to the principle.
‘Fasād al-i'tibār’ is a specific defect in analogical reasoning.
2
Understand the claim
Al-Tūfī asserts that if fasād al-i'tibār is proven against the mustadill, his argument becomes invalid because his claim lacks a valid proof.
The objection is 'absolute', meaning it is not limited to one part.
3
Recognize the reasoning
The ruling (ḥukm) is built on the proof. If the proof is defective, the ruling has no foundation.
This is the logical link: invalid proof → invalid argument.
4
Apply in dialectical context
In a debate, if the objector successfully demonstrates fasād al-i'tibār, the arguer's position collapses entirely.
No partial salvaging is allowed according to al-Tūfī.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا، ويرى الإمام الطوفي أنه إذا توجه سؤال فساد الاعتبار على المستدل، مطلقا، لتجرد دعواه عن دليله، أي أنه إذا ثبت سقوط دليل المستدل ومخالفته لأصول أقوى منه، فإن الاستدلال يعتبر باطلا، فيسقط ما بني عليه، وما ذلك إلا لأن فساد الاعتبار قادح في عمدة الحكم، وهو الدليل
This, and Imam al-Tūfī holds that if the question of invalidity of consideration is raised against the arguer (mustadill), absolutely, because his claim is bereft of proof, i.e., if it is established that the arguer's proof has fallen and it contradicts stronger principles, then the argument is considered invalid, so what is based on it falls away; and that is only because fasād al-i'tibār is a defect in the pillar of the ruling, which is the proof.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Tūfī's ruling on fasād al-i'tibār. He asserts that once the objection of invalid analogy is raised and the proof (dalīl) of the arguer is shown to be invalid or contradictory to stronger principles, the entire argument collapses. The reason is that the validity of the ruling (ḥukm) rests on the proof; if the proof is defective, the ruling has no foundation. This is a key principle in jadal: the strength of an argument depends on the soundness of its evidence.

Key Terms
فساد الاعتبار
مستدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Tūfī, what happens if a question of fasād al-i'tibār is directed at the mustadill?
A) The argument is partially revised.
B) The argument becomes invalid and its basis collapses.
C) The mustadill can present a new proof.
D) The ruling is still valid.

Key Takeaway
Fasād al-i'tibār is a fatal defect: if the proof is corrupt, the entire argument falls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a hypothetical debate, a mustadill claims that qiyās permits a certain transaction because it is similar to a permissible contract. An opponent objects that the analogy contradicts a stronger textual principle. According to al-Tūfī, what is the outcome? Explain in one sentence.
?
Identify a scenario from usul al-fiqh where an argument might be invalidated due to fasād al-i'tibār. Name the original ruling, the analogy, and the stronger principle it contradicts.

Objection by Counterexample (Naqḍ) in Legal Dialectic
Defines naqḍ with examples from fasting and theft.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a naqḍ objection and construct your own counterexample in a legal debate.

Core Idea
Naqḍ: the cause exists, but the ruling does not – a broken promise.

In Islamic legal dialectic, objecting to the opponent's effective cause ('illa) is a key skill. One powerful method is naqḍ, where you show a case where the alleged cause is present, yet the ruling is absent. This lesson walks through the definition and a concrete example from the debate on fasting intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alleged cause and ruling
In the example, the Shafi'i claims that lacking intention at dawn (cause) makes the fast invalid (ruling). Find these elements in the passage.
The cause is 'bareness of the beginning of the fast from intention'; the ruling is 'invalidity of the fast'.
2
Find the counterexample
The Hanafi objects using voluntary fast (ṣawm al-taṭawwu'), where one may intend during the day. Here, the cause (no intention at dawn) exists, but the ruling (invalidity) does not.
Voluntary fast is valid even without pre-dawn intention.
3
Explain why the counterexample refutes the cause
Since the cause is present but the ruling is absent, the claimed causal relationship is broken. Thus, the Shafi'i's cause is not the true 'illa.
4
Assess the validity and anticipate a response
The Hanafi's objection is a valid naqḍ. The Shafi'i may respond by distinguishing obligatory vs. voluntary fast, but that would require a refined cause.
In dialectic, the burden shifts to the original arguer to defend the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني: الاعتراض على العلة بالنقض وهو إبداء العلة بدون الحكم، أي أن لا تكون العلة مطابقة للحكم أن يبين المعترض وجود الوصف المدعى كونه علة في محل، مع الحكم عنه في ذلك المحل، مثل أن يقول الشافعي في من ترك النية في الصوم ليلا تعرى أول صومه عن النية، فلا يصح؛ فيجعل العلة في عدم صحة الصوم عراء أول الصوم عن النية وخلوه عنها، فيقول له الحنفي معترضا: هذه العلة منقوضة بصوم التطوع، فإنه صحيح إذا أحدث النية فيه نهارا . . وبذلك تكون العلة - وهي عراء أول الصوم عن النية - قد وجدت في صوم التطوع، وتخلف الحكم، وهو عدم صحة الصوم عنه، لأن الصوم في هذه الحالة ومثاله أيضًا أن يقول المستدل في مسألة النباش أنه سرق نصابًا كاملا من حرز
The second topic: Objection to the cause by counterexample (naqḍ). It is presenting the cause without the ruling, i.e., that the cause does not conform to the ruling, meaning the objector shows the existence of the attribute claimed to be the cause in a case, yet the ruling is absent in that case. For example, when the Shafi'i says about one who omitted the intention (niyya) for fasting at night, the beginning of his fast is bare of intention, so it is not valid; thus he makes the cause for invalidity of the fast the bareness of the beginning of the fast of intention. Then the Hanafi objects: this cause is refuted by the voluntary fast (ṣawm al-taṭawwu'), for it is valid if one initiates the intention during the day... Thus the cause (bareness of the beginning of intention) exists in the voluntary fast, but the ruling (invalidity) is absent. And another example: when the arguer says about the grave robber (nabbāsh) that he stole a complete nisāb from a guarded place
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage introduces the second method of objection in dialectic: naqḍ (counterexample). The objector shows that the alleged cause exists in a different case, but the ruling does not follow. Thus, the claimed cause is not truly the cause. The first example: The Shafi'i argues that the fast is invalid if the intention is absent at the beginning of the day (i.e., before dawn). The cause is the 'bareness' of the beginning from intention. The Hanafi objects using voluntary fast (ṣawm al-taṭawwu') which does not require intention before dawn; one can intend during the day. So the cause exists (no intention at dawn) but the ruling (invalidity) is absent (the fast is valid). Thus the cause is invalid. The second example, though incomplete, would show that the grave robber (nabbāsh) taking a complete nisāb from a grave (considered a guarded place) might be analogized to theft, but the cause may be refuted by a counterexample.

Key Terms
نقض
علة
حكم
صوم التطوع
نباش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the definition of naqḍ (objection by counterexample) as given in the passage?
A) Showing that the cause is absent when the ruling is present.
B) Showing that the cause is present but the ruling is absent.
C) Showing that the ruling is present without a cause.
D) Showing that the cause is present in all cases.

Key Takeaway
Naqḍ refutes a claimed cause by presenting a case where the cause exists but the ruling does not, forcing a revision of the cause.

Distinguishing Hadd from Qisas in Dialectical Argument
Author distinguishes hadd from qisas using conditions of chastity and religious status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the conditions for hadd and qisas using the example of slander and killing, and apply this distinction in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Hadd and qisas have different conditions — hadd requires chastity, qisas does not.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key distinction between the fixed punishment (hadd) for slander and the retaliation (qisas) for killing. Understanding this difference is crucial for constructing valid analogical arguments in fiqh and defending positions in jadal. The author uses a specific example to show that the two punishments operate under different conditions, which affects how they can be used as precedents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two punishments being compared
Find the terms 'hadd' and 'qisas' in the Arabic and note the specific context of slander (qadhf) and killing (qatl).
2
Extract the condition for hadd
The passage says hadd is obligatory due to chastity and righteousness (al-iffa wa al-salah). Note that a sinful Muslim cannot claim hadd.
3
Extract the condition for qisas
Qisas is not permissible for a disbeliever against a Muslim even if the crime occurred during disbelief, but killing (qatl) is exacted for a disbeliever against a Muslim if the crime occurred during disbelief. So qisas has a condition regarding the status of the claimant.
4
Compare the conditions
Contrast the two: hadd requires the claimant to be chaste; qisas requires that the claimant is a Muslim (or the crime occurred while the victim was Muslim). This shows they differ.
5
Apply the distinction in a jadal argument
If an opponent tries to analogize hadd and qisas, you can point out the different conditions to refute the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحد بقذف المسلم، ولا يثبت للكافر على المسلم حق ويثبت للمسلم، والجواب أن يبين أن حكم القتل مخالف للحد والاقتصاص، ألا ترى أن الحد يجب بالعفة والصلاح ولا يجب للمسلم الفاسق، بخلاف القتل فإنه يجب للمسلم الفاسق على المسلم العفيف، وأما الاقتصاص فلا يجوز أن يستوفيه الكافر من المسلم وإن ثبت له عليه حال الكفر، بخلاف القتل فإنه يستوفى للكافر من المسلم حال الإسلام إذا ثبت له عليه حال الكفر، فافترقا. هذا، ويرى الإمام الطوفي أنه إذا توجه سؤال فساد الاعتبار على المستدل، مطلقا، لتجرد دعواه عن دليله، أي أنه إذا ثبت سقوط دليل المستدل ومخالفته لأصول أقوى منه، فإن الاستدلال يعتبر باطلا، فيسقط ما بني عليه، وما ذلك إلا لأن فساد الاعتبار قادح في عمدة الحكم، وهو الدليل كما عبر انقطع الطوفي
And [the hadd] is prescribed by accusing a Muslim of adultery, and the disbeliever does not have a right against the Muslim while the Muslim does have [a right]. The answer is to clarify that the ruling of killing is different from hadd and qisas. Do you not see that the hadd is obligatory due to chastity and righteousness and is not obligatory for the sinful Muslim, unlike killing which is obligatory for the sinful Muslim against the chaste Muslim. As for qisas, it is not permissible for the disbeliever to exact it from the Muslim even if it was established against him during disbelief, unlike killing which is exacted for the disbeliever from the Muslim during Islam if it was established against him during disbelief. Thus they differ. And Imam al-Tufi holds that if a question of corruption of consideration (fasad al-i'tibar) is directed at the argument-proponent, absolutely, due to his claim being stripped of evidence, i.e., if it is established that the proponent's evidence has fallen and conflicts with stronger principles, then the inference is considered invalid, and what is built upon it falls. That is only because corruption of consideration is a defect in the pillar of the ruling, which is the evidence, as he expressed. Al-Tufi was cut off.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage addresses two separate but related points. First, it distinguishes the penalty of hadd (fixed punishment) from qisas (retaliation), using the example of slander (qadhf). The author explains that a Muslim can be subject to hadd for slander, but a non-Muslim cannot claim a right against a Muslim in this regard, whereas a Muslim can claim against a non-Muslim. The key difference is that hadd requires the plaintiff to be chaste and upright (al-iffa wa al-salah), so a sinful Muslim cannot claim hadd against another Muslim, but the ruling of qatl (killing) is different: a sinful Muslim can claim qisas against a chaste Muslim if the latter committed murder. Similarly, qisas cannot be exacted by a non-Muslim from a Muslim even if the crime occurred while the non-Muslim was in disbelief? Actually the text says qisas cannot be exacted by a disbeliever from a Muslim, but qatl can be exacted for the disbeliever from the Muslim if the crime was committed during disbelief. This shows that the two types of punishment have different conditions. Second, the passage introduces Imam al-Tufi's view on fasad al-i'tibar (corruption of consideration). He argues that if the opponent shows that the proponent's evidence has collapsed or conflicts with stronger principles, then the entire inference is invalid, because the evidence is the foundation of the ruling. This is a key principle in dialectic.

قصاص
عفة

A) When the accused is a non-Muslim

D) When the punishment is qisas

Key Takeaway
Hadd and qisas have distinct conditions — hadd requires the plaintiff to be chaste, while qisas does not depend on the plaintiff's moral status, which prevents blanket analogies between them.

Imam al-Tufi's Principle of Fasad al-I'tibar (Corruption of Consideration)
Author presents Imam al-Tufi's view on fasad al-i'tibar as a principle for invalidating arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Imam al-Tufi's view that when evidence is shown to be invalid or contradictory to stronger principles, the entire inference is considered corrupted, and apply this principle in a jadal argument.

Core Idea
If the evidence collapses, the whole argument falls — that is fasad al-i'tibar.

Imam al-Tufi introduces a fundamental principle in jadal: if a proponent's evidence is shown to be unsupported or conflicting with stronger principles, then the inference built on that evidence is invalid. This concept, known as fasad al-i'tibar (corruption of consideration), is a powerful tool for refuting arguments. In this passage, the author presents al-Tufi's view and explains why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Imam al-Tufi holds that if a question of fasad al-i'tibar is raised against a proponent's argument, the argument is invalid.
2
Understand the cause
The cause is that the proponent's claim is stripped of evidence (tajarrud da'wahu 'an dalilihi) or the evidence conflicts with stronger principles.
3
Recognize the consequence
The inference (istidlal) is considered invalid, and whatever was built upon it falls.
4
Grasp the rationale
Because the evidence is the 'pillar of the ruling' ('umdat al-hukm); if it is corrupted, the ruling cannot stand.
5
Apply in a jadal scenario
If you are in a debate and you can show that your opponent's evidence is weak or contradicts stronger principles, you can argue fasad al-i'tibar to invalidate his entire argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Cause for Obligation and the First Rebuttal Method
Defines the cause for obligation and explains the first rebuttal method: denying the objection's premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a cause for obligation (illa mawdu'a lil-wujub) and apply the first rebuttal method (man‘ mas'alat al-naqd) in a jadal exchange on a fiqh issue.

Core Idea
If a cause exists without its ruling, the objection stands — rebut it by denying the objection's premise.

In this lesson, we explore the second major type of cause in jadal: the cause established for obligation. The key condition is that if the cause exists without the ruling, the cause is invalidated. When an opponent points out such a counterexample (naqd), the first way to respond is to deny the issue of the objection itself. This lesson unpacks two strategies for that rebuttal, drawn from real usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause for obligation in an argument
In the Hanafi example, the cause is 'purification' (طهارة). The ruling claimed is 'does not require intention'. The opponent cites tayammum as a counterexample where purification exists but intention is required. This is the naqd.
2
Detect the condition for invalidation
The condition: if the cause exists without the ruling, the cause is invalid. Confirm that the opponent's objection shows the cause (purification) without the claimed ruling (no intention).
3
Understand the first rebuttal method: man‘ mas'alat al-naqd
The respondent does not concede the objection. He can either (a) claim his school has two opinions and his proof is based on one, or (b) claim there is no textual basis for the objection.
4
Apply the method to the examples
In the Maliki divorce exception example, the Shafi'i objects using the case of conditioning Hajj. The Maliki rebuts by saying there are two narrations, and his proof is based on one. In the Shafi'i commodity destruction example, the Hanafi objects with rescission; the Shafi'i says rescission is permissible in his school.
5
Distinguish the second sub-type
When the respondent says 'I do not know any text on this', he is denying the factual basis of the objection. The Maliki on intention in wudu uses this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلة الموضوعة لإثبات الأحكام في الأعيان: وهي على نوعين: علة موضوعة للوجوب، وعلة موضوعة للجواز.
العلة الموضوعة للوجوب: هذه العلة متى وجدت دون الحكم، كان ذلك نقضا لها، وذلك مثل أن يقول الحنفي: إن الوضوء طهارة، فلا يفتقر إلى النية، كإزالة النجاسة، فينتقض عليه بالتيمم، لأنه طهارة ويفتقر إلى النية فإذا ما اعترض معترض على قياس يستند إلى علة من هذا القبيل، كان الطريق في دفع نقضه من ثلاثة أوجه:
الجواب الأول: منع مسألة النقض:
وهو نوعان:
أحدهما: أن يقول لا أسلم هذه المسألة فإن المذهب عندي خلاف هذا وذلك إذا كان للمستدل في مذهبه قولان فلا يسلم أحدهما؛ مثاله أن يستدل المالكي على أن الاستثناء لا يرفع حكم الطلاق بأن هذا استثناء يرفع حكم الطلاق في الحال والمآل فوجب أن يبطل، كما لو قال: أنت طالق ثلاثا إلا ثلاثاً، فيقول الشافعي معترضا: ينتقض به إذا اشترط مشيئة الحج، فإن هذا المعنى موجود فيه، ومع ذلك فإنه لا يبطل الاستثناء، والجواب عنه أن يقول: عن مالك فيه روايتان إحداهما أن الاستثناء يبطل كالاستثناء بمشيئة الله تعالى، وعلى هذه الرواية بنينا الدليل فلا يلزمنا هذا النقض ومثاله أيضًا: أن يقرر شافعي في المخالف عند هلاك السلعة: إنه فسخ بيع يصح مع رد العين فيصح مع رد القيمة، كما لو اشترى ثوبا بعبد وتقابضا، ثم هلك العبد ووجد مشتري الثوب بالثوب عيبا . فيقول الحنفي ناقضًا: هذا يبطل بالإقالة . فيقول الشافعي مجيبا: لا أسلم النقض بالإقالة، فإنها تجوز عندي مع هلاك السلعة والثاني: أن يقول لا أعرف في هذا نصا، ولا يلزمني النقض»، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على اعتبار النية في الوضوء بأن هذه طهارة تتعدى محل موجبها
The cause established for affirming rulings in particular instances: and it is of two types: a cause established for obligation, and a cause established for permissibility.
The cause established for obligation: Whenever this cause is found without the ruling, that is an invalidation of it. For example, if a Hanafi says: 'Wudu is a purification, so it does not require intention, like removing filth,' then it is invalidated against him by tayammum, because it is a purification and requires intention. So when an opponent objects to a syllogism that is based on this kind of cause, the way to rebut his objection is through three methods:
The first answer: Denying the issue of the objection.
And it is of two types:
One of them: that he says, 'I do not concede this issue, for my school has a different view on this.' This is if the arguer has two opinions in his school, so he does not concede one of them. An example: a Maliki argues that exception (istithna') does not remove the ruling of divorce, because this is an exception that removes the ruling of divorce in the present and future, so it must be nullified, as if he said: 'You are divorced three times except three.' Then a Shafi'i objects: 'It is invalidated by the case where one conditions the will of Hajj, for this meaning is found in it, yet the exception is not nullified.' The answer is that he says: 'There are two narrations from Malik on this: one that the exception is nullified, like exception by the will of Allah, and upon this narration we built our proof, so this objection does not bind us.' Another example: a Shafi'i argues in the case of the destruction of the commodity: 'It is a rescission of sale that is valid with the return of the object, so it is valid with the return of the value, like if someone bought a garment with a slave and they took possession, then the slave perished and the buyer of the garment found a defect in the garment.' Then a Hanafi objects: 'This is invalidated by rescission (iqala).' The Shafi'i answers: 'I do not concede the objection by rescission, for it is permissible in my view with the destruction of the commodity.' The second type: that he says, 'I do not know any text on this, and the objection does not bind me.' For example, a Maliki argues for the validity of intention in wudu because this is a purification that extends beyond the place of its requirement.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major type of cause: the cause established for affirming rulings in particular instances, which is then divided into a cause for obligation (wujub) and a cause for permissibility (jawaz). It focuses on the cause for obligation, giving the condition that if the cause exists without the ruling, the cause is invalidated. A Hanafi example on wudu and intention illustrates this: the Hanafi claims wudu does not require intention by analogy with removing filth, but the opponent cites tayammum as a counterexample where purification requires intention. The author then states that when such an objection arises, there are three methods to rebut it, and only the first is detailed here: denying the issue of the objection (man' mas'alat al-naqd). This method has two sub-types. The first sub-type is when the arguer has two opinions in his school; he can say he does not concede the objection because his proof is based on one of the two opinions. Two examples are given: (1) a Maliki on exception in divorce, where the Shafi'i objects using the case of conditioning the will of Hajj; the Maliki rebuts by citing two narrations from Malik. (2) A Shafi'i on the destruction of a commodity, where the Hanafi objects using rescission; the Shafi'i rebuts by saying rescission is permissible in his school. The second sub-type is when the arguer says he knows no textual basis for the objection, so it does not bind him. An example is given of a Maliki arguing for intention in wudu based on the nature of purification; the opponent's objection is not recognized because there is no known text supporting it.

Key Terms
علة موضوعة للوجوب
نقض
منع مسألة النقض
استثناء
إقالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Hanafi argues: 'Wudu is a purification, so it does not require intention, like removing filth.' An opponent objects using tayammum. According to the passage, why does the Hanafi's cause for obligation face this objection?
A) Because the Hanafi's cause is not a valid cause for obligation.
B) Because the cause (purification) exists in tayammum, but the ruling (no intention) does not.
C) Because tayammum is not a purification.
D) Because the Hanafi denied the issue of the objection.

Key Takeaway
When a cause for obligation is used in qiyas, any counterexample where the cause exists without the ruling invalidates the cause; the first rebuttal is to deny the counterexample's validity within your school.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar debate: A Hanbali argues that selling dates before they ripen is invalid because it is a sale of non-existent object, like selling unborn animals. An opponent objects that selling fruits before they ripen is permitted in some schools. Apply the first rebuttal method to defend the Hanbali position using a school opinion.

Denying the Premise with a Different School Opinion
First subtype of denial: using different school opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a proponent can deny an objection's premise by appealing to a different school opinion, and illustrate it with an example from Islamic legal debate.

Core Idea
Denying a premise requires a basis in the school's own divergent narrations.

In Islamic dialectical reasoning, when an objector raises a counterexample (naqd) against a legal analogy based on a cause (ʿilla), the proponent's first permissible response is to deny the premise of the objection. However, this denial is not arbitrary; it must be grounded in the proponent's own school having two conflicting opinions on the matter. The proponent simply adheres to the opinion that does not lead to the objection, while the other opinion is known but not binding. This lesson explores the first subtype of this defense with concrete examples from the Mālikī and Shāfiʿī schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's premise
Find the specific counterexample (naqd) that the objector presents. In the first example, the Shāfiʿī objects that if a Mālikī argues that exception (istithnāʾ) in divorce invalidates the pronouncement, then stipulating the will of Hajj should also invalidate it, but it does not.
The objector's premise is that the cause (ʿilla) exists in both cases, so the ruling should follow uniformly.
2
Check the proponent's school for divergent opinions
The proponent (Mālikī) must verify whether his school has more than one opinion on the issue. Here, there are two narrations from Mālik: one supports the proponent's argument, the other does not. The proponent chooses the narration that aligns with his proof.
If the school has only one opinion, this type of denial is not available.
3
Formulate the denial
The proponent says: 'I do not accept this objection because in my school there is a narration (or opinion) that contradicts the premise of the objection. I built my proof on that narration.'
This shifts the debate to which narration is stronger, but the immediate objection is deflected.
4
Analyze the second example (Shāfiʿī vs Ḥanafī on rescission)
The Shāfiʿī argues that rescission of sale with returning the value is valid because it is like rescission with returning the ʿayn. The Ḥanafī objects by analogy to revocation (iqāla) where the commodity must be returned. The Shāfiʿī denies the premise: in his school, iqāla is valid even if the commodity is destroyed, so the objection does not stand.
The proponent's school's position must be clearly different from the objector's assumption.

Source Text

فإذا ما اعترض معترض على قياس يستند إلى علة من هذا القبيل، كان الطريق في دفع نقضه من ثلاثة أوجه : الجواب الأول : منع مسألة النقض : وهو نوعان : أحدهما : أن يقول لا أسلم هذه المسألة فإن المذهب عندي خلاف هذا وذلك إذا كان للمستدل في مذهبه قولان فلا يسلم أحدهما؛ مثاله أن يستدل المالكي على أن الاستثناء لا يرفع حكم الطلاق بأن هذا استثناء يرفع حكم الطلاق في الحال والمآل فوجب أن يبطل، كما لو قال : أنت طالق ثلاثا إلا ثلاثاً، فيقول الشافعي معترضا : ينتقض به إذا اشترط مشيئة الحج، فإن هذا المعنى موجود فيه، ومع ذلك فإنه لا يبطل الاستثناء، والجواب عنه أن يقول : عن مالك فيه روايتان إحداهما أن الاستثناء يبطل كالاستثناء بمشيئة الله تعالى، وعلى هذه الرواية بنينا الدليل فلا يلزمنا هذا النقض ومثاله أيضًا : أن يقرر شافعي في المخالف عند هلاك السلعة : إنه فسخ بيع يصح مع رد العين فيصح مع رد القيمة، كما لو اشترى ثوبا بعبد وتقابضا، ثم هلك العبد ووجد مشتري الثوب بالثوب عيبا . فيقول الحنفي ناقضًا : هذا يبطل بالإقالة . فيقول الشافعي مجيبا : لا أسلم النقض بالإقالة، فإنها تجوز عندي مع هلاك السلعة والثاني : أن يقول لا أعرف في هذا نصا، ولا يلزمني النقض»، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على اعتبار النية في الوضوء بأن هذه طهارة تتعدى محل موجبها، فافتقرت إلى النية كالتيمم فيقول الحنفي : هذا ينتقض بغسل الإناء من ولوغ الكلب، فإنها طهارة تتعدى محل موجبها ولا تفتقر إلى النية، فيقول المالكي مجيبا : ليس لمالك في هذا نص ويحتمل ألا نسلم فلا يلزمني ذلك
When an objector objects to a qiyās based on a ʿilla of this kind, the way to repel his objection is from three aspects: The first answer: denial of the objection's premise. It is of two types: One of them: That he says, 'I do not accept this premise, for my school holds contrary to this.' This is when the proponent has two opinions in his school, so he does not accept one of them. Its example: The Mālikī argues that exception (istithnāʾ) does not remove the ruling of divorce, because this exception removes the ruling of divorce temporarily and eventually, so it should be invalid, as if he said: 'You are thrice divorced except three,' then the Shāfiʿī objects: 'It is rebutted by the case when he stipulates the will of Hajj, for this meaning exists therein, yet the exception does not invalidate.' The answer to it is that he says: 'There are two narrations from Mālik on this; one of them is that the exception is invalid like the exception by the will of Allah. On this narration we built our proof, so this objection does not apply to us.' Another example: The Shāfiʿī argues with the opponent in the case of the destruction of the commodity: it is a rescission of a sale that is valid with returning the ʿayn, so it is valid with returning the value, as if he bought a garment with a slave and they seized, then the slave perished and the buyer of the garment found a defect in the garment. Then the Ḥanafī objects: 'This is invalid by revocation (iqāla).' Then the Shāfiʿī responds: 'I do not accept the objection by revocation, for it is permissible in my view with the destruction of the commodity.' The second type: That he says, 'I know no text (naṣṣ) on this, and the objection does not bind me.' An example: The Mālikī argues for the consideration of intention in wuḍūʾ because it is a purification that transcends the place of its obligation, so it requires intention like tayammum. Then the Ḥanafī says: 'This is rebutted by washing the vessel from the dog's licking, for it is a purification that transcends the place of its obligation and does not require intention.' Then the Mālikī responds: 'There is no text for Mālik on this, and it is possible that we do not accept it, so it does not bind me.'
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the first answer to an objection in Islamic dialectical reasoning (jadal). The author describes the method of 'denial of the objection's premise' (manʿ masʾalat al-naqd). He first sets the context: when an objector raises a counterexample against a qiyās based on a ʿilla, the proponent can deflect it in three ways, and the first is to deny the correctness of the premise of the objection. This answer has two subtypes. The first subtype: the proponent says, 'I do not accept this premise because my school holds a different position.' This applies when the proponent's school has two conflicting opinions; he simply does not accept the one that the objection relies on. The author gives an example from the Mālikī school: the Mālikī argues that an exception (istithnāʾ) in divorce does not remove the ruling of divorce. The Shāfiʿī objects by comparing it to stipulating the will of Allah in Hajj, where exception does not void the act. The Mālikī responds that there are two narrations from Malik: one that the exception invalidates, and on that narration he built his proof, so the objection does not apply. Another example: the Shāfiʿī defends a position on rescission of sale, and the Ḥanafī objects by analogizing to revocation (iqāla). The Shāfiʿī simply denies the premise, stating that in his school iqāla is permissible even with destruction of the commodity, so the objection fails. The second subtype: the proponent says, 'I know no scriptural text (naṣṣ) on this issue, so the objection does not bind me.' This is used when his school has no recorded position. The example: the Mālikī argues that intention is required in wuḍūʾ by analogy to tayammum. The Ḥanafī objects by citing washing a vessel from a dog's lick, which also transcends its cause but does not require intention. The Mālikī responds that there is no text from Mālik on this matter, so he is not bound by the objection. The author's treatment shows that the proponent's response must be grounded either in his school's differing opinion or in the absence of textual evidence; it is not a mere refusal without basis.

D) The proponent can prove the objection's premise is false in reality

Key Takeaway
Denying the premise of an objection requires a legitimate basis: either a differing opinion within the proponent's school or the absence of a recorded text on the matter.

Denying the Premise for Lack of Text
Second subtype of denial: lack of text (naṣṣ) on the issue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second subtype of denying the objection's premise (lack of text) and distinguish it from the first subtype, using an example from Islamic legal debate.

Core Idea
If the school has no textual evidence on the point, the objection cannot bind.

In Islamic dialectical reasoning, when an objector raises a counterexample against a legal analogy, the proponent can sometimes reply that his school has no scriptural text (naṣṣ) on that particular issue. This is not an admission of ignorance but a principled limitation: the objector cannot force a ruling where the school holds no transmitted position. This lesson explores this second subtype through the Mālikī-Ḥanafī exchange on intention in wuḍūʾ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's proposed counterexample
In the example, the Mālikī argues that intention is required in wuḍūʾ because it is a purification that transcends its cause, analogous to tayammum. The Ḥanafī objects: 'This is rebutted by washing a vessel from a dog's licking, for that is also a purification that transcends its cause but does not require intention.'
The objector is using the same ʿilla to argue that the ruling should not follow uniformly.
2
Determine if the proponent's school has a text (naṣṣ) on this specific case
The Mālikī checks his school's corpus: is there a narrated ruling from Mālik about washing a vessel from a dog's licking and whether intention is required? He concludes there is no text on this point.
If there were a text, he would be bound by it; its absence gives him room to deny the premise.
3
Formulate the denial of the premise
The Mālikī says: 'I know of no text from Mālik on this matter, and it is possible that we do not accept it, so this objection does not bind me.' This allows him to maintain his original qiyās.
The phrase 'it is possible that we do not accept it' indicates that without a text, the school is not committed to a position, so the objector's counterexample is not a valid refutation.
4
Contrast with the first subtype
In the first subtype, the school has multiple opinions and the proponent chooses one. Here, the school has no opinion at all. Both are ways to deny the premise, but they rely on different factual bases.
This distinction is important for selecting the correct jadal response.

Repelling the Objection by Denying the ʿIlla
Author explains the second way to rebut an objection: denying the cause, with two subtypes and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two subtypes of repelling an objection by denying the ʿilla and apply each in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Denying the ʿilla is a powerful jadal move to escape an objection without abandoning your school.

In legal debate, an objector often attacks by citing a counterexample that should share the cause of your ruling. The second classical way to respond is to deny that the counterexample shares the same effective cause (ʿilla). This lesson unpacks the two subtypes: one where your school has a recorded position on the counterexample, and one where you rely on a revealed-law principle. Mastering this technique gives you a precise tool to defend your school's stance without conceding the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's qiyas and its claimed ʿilla
Find the original analogy: what is the proponent's ruling, what is the asl, and what is the ʿilla that connects them? For example, Hanafi argues rinsing the mouth is wajib in ghusl al-janaba because it is a limb that must be purified from impurity, just like other limbs.
2
Locate the objector's counterexample
The objector provides a case that, according to him, shares the ʿilla but does not have the same ruling. For instance, the inside of the eyes also require purification from impurity (ʿilla) but are not washed in ghusl al-janaba. This is the naqd.
3
Check for a recorded position (madhhab masṭūr) on the counterexample
If the proponent's school has a recorded position that the counterexample does NOT share the ruling (e.g., Hanafi holds that inside eyes are not wajib to wash from impurity), then the proponent can say 'That doesn't bind me' – denying the ʿilla based on his school's stance.
The objector may try to prove the counterexample does share the ʿilla using a separate argument, but the burden shifts to him.
4
If no recorded position, use a sharʿī principle
When the proponent has no textual position, he can repel the objection by referencing a revealed principle that distinguishes the counterexample. Example: Maliki denies marriage is a contract on benefit because it is a contract on permissibility – thus the ʿilla of 'contract on benefit' does not apply.
The objector must first prove that the counterexample shares the ʿilla, otherwise the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب الثاني : دفع النقض بمنع العلة : وهو أيضًا على ضربين : الأول : أن يكون للمستدل فيها مذهب مسطور : وذلك مثل أن يستدل الحنفي على وجوب المضمضة والاستنشاق في غسل الجنابة، بأنه عضو يجب تطهيره من النجاسة، فوجب غسله من الجنابة كسائر الأعضاء، فيقول له المالكي أو الشافعي : هذا ينتقض بداخل العينين، فإنه يجب من النجاسة ولا يجب من الجنابة، فيقول له الحنفي مجيبا : لا يلزمني هذا، لأن داخل العينين عندي لا يجب غسله من النجاسة، فلا يلزمني النقض فهذا الجواب منع للنقض، ولا يجوز للمعترض أن يستدل على أن العينين يجب غسلهما من النجاسة ليلزم النقض، ولكنه يجوز له أن يقيس أن النقض يلزمه بأن يقول : لا خلاف بيني وبينك أنه لو اكتحل بالبول لوجب عليه تطهيره من النجاسة، إما بغسل وإما بمسح وإنما الخلاف بيننا في صفة التطهير الثاني : أن لا يكون للمستدل في المسألة مذهب مسطور : فهنا يمكن أن يدفع النقض بما طريقه الشرع، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن الأجرة لا تجب بعقد الإجارة، بأنه عقد على المنفعة، فلا يجب تعجيل العوض بنفس العقد كالمضاربة، فيقول الشافعي : هذا ينتقض بالنكاح، فيقول المستدل : النكاح ليس بعقد على منفعة، وإنما هو عقد على الحل والإباحة ولكي يلزم النقض لابد للمعترض أن يثبت - إن استطاع - أن النكاح عقد على منفعة، وإلا فإن النقض لا يكون لازما للمستدل
The second answer: repelling the objection by denying the ʿilla. It is also of two types: The first: That the proponent has a recorded position in the matter. Example: The Ḥanafī argues for the obligation of rinsing the mouth and sniffling in ghusl al-Janāba, because it is a limb that must be purified from impurity, so it is obligatory to wash it from janāba like other limbs. Then the Mālikī or Shāfiʿī says: 'This is rebutted by the inside of the eyes, for it is obligatory from impurity but not from janāba.' Then the Ḥanafī responds: 'This does not bind me, because in my view the inside of the eyes is not obligatory to wash from impurity, so the objection does not apply.' So this answer is denial of the objection, and it is not permissible for the objector to argue that the eyes must be washed from impurity in order to bind the objection, but it is permissible for him to analogize that the objection binds him by saying: 'There is no dispute between me and you that if he uses kohl with urine, he must purify it from impurity, either by washing or by wiping; the difference between us is only in the mode of purification.' The second type: That the proponent has no recorded position in the matter. Here, it is possible to repel the objection by what is based on Sharʿ. Example: The Mālikī argues that hire (ujra) is not obligatory by the contract of lease, because it is a contract on benefit, so immediate payment of the price is not obligatory by the contract itself like in muḍāraba. Then the Shāfiʿī says: 'This is rebutted by marriage.' Then the proponent says: 'Marriage is not a contract on benefit; rather it is a contract on permission and permissibility. For the objection to bind, the objector must prove—if he can—that marriage is a contract on benefit; otherwise, the objection is not binding on the proponent.'
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage explains the second answer to an objection: repelling the objection by denying the legal cause (ʿilla). The author divides it into two types. The first type is when the proponent has a recorded position (madhhab masṭūr) on the matter. He gives an example from the Hanafi school: arguing that rinsing the mouth and nostrils is obligatory in ghusl al-janaba because the mouth is a limb that must be cleansed from impurity, analogously to other limbs. The objector (Maliki or Shafi'i) rebuts by citing the inside of the eyes, which requires purification from impurity but not from janaba. The Hanafi responds by denying that the inside of the eyes requires washing from impurity in his school, so the objection does not hold. The author notes that the objector may not simply assume his own position; he is allowed to try to prove by analogy that the inside of the eyes must be washed, but if he cannot, the objection is defeated. This shows the nuance in jadal: the objector can attempt to establish the premise through a separate argument. The second type occurs when the proponent has no recorded position on the issue. In such cases, he can rebut the objection using a Sharʿī principle. The example: the Maliki argues that hire payment is not obligatory by the contract of lease itself, analogizing to mudaraba. The Shafi'i objects by analogizing to marriage, which also involves benefit. The Maliki responds that marriage is not a contract on benefit but on permission, so the objector must first prove that marriage is a contract on benefit for the objection to be binding. The author thus illustrates how the proponent defends by denying the causal sameness.

Key Terms
دفع النقض بمنع العلة
علة
مذهب مسطور
شرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second type of repelling the objection by denying the ʿilla, when the proponent has no recorded position, on what basis can he repel the objection?
A) By denying the premise of the objection
B) By using a Sharʿī principle
C) By claiming his school has no text
D) By rejecting the analogy entirely

Key Takeaway
Repelling by denying the cause (manʿ al-ʿilla) allows the proponent to escape an objection by showing that the counterexample does not share the same legal cause, either due to a recorded position (madhhab masṭūr) or by a sharʿī principle.

Denying the Cause (Man' al-'Illa) in Dialectical Refutation
Author explains the second method of refuting objections by denying the effective cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the strategy of denying the cause (man' al-'illa) when faced with an objection in a dialectical exchange, distinguishing between cases where the arguer has a recorded position and where he does not.

Core Idea
To deny the cause is to block an objection by showing it does not apply — but only if your school really holds that view.

In dialectical debates (jadal), when an opponent cites a counterexample to your argument, you do not always have to accept it. One powerful response is to deny that the cause (al-'illa) of your ruling applies to that counterexample. This lesson unpacks the second method of refutation taught in the Islamic logical tradition: daf' al-naqd bi-man' al-'illa. We will examine how jurists like the Hanafis and Malikis used this strategy, and learn to distinguish two subtypes: where the arguer has a recorded position (madhhab masṭūr) and where he does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's claim and its 'illa
In the passage, the Hanafi claims rinsing is obligatory in janaba because it is a body part that must be purified from impurity. The 'illa is 'being a body part that must be purified from impurity.'
2
Identify the objector's counterexample
The objector cites the inside of the eyes, which must be purified from impurity (e.g., from najasa) but not from janaba. This suggests the 'illa is not sufficient.
3
Examine the arguer's response: deny the cause
The Hanafi responds by denying that the inside of the eyes must be purified from impurity according to his school. Thus he blocks the objection without accepting the counterexample.
This is man' al-'illa.
4
Check for a recorded position (madhhab masṭūr)
The Hanafi's response is valid because he has a recorded position (his school's ruling) that the inside of the eyes is not subject to purification from impurity. If no such recorded position exists, the arguer must use other means (shar' or custom) to deny the cause.
5
Test with the second subtype
In the Maliki's argument about wages, the objector cites marriage. The Maliki denies that marriage is a contract for usufruct, so the cause does not apply. Here the arguer has no recorded position, so he must rely on legal definition or custom.
This illustrates the second subtype.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب الثاني : دفع النقض بمنع العلة : وهو أيضًا على ضربين : الأول : أن يكون للمستدل فيها مذهب مسطور : وذلك مثل أن يستدل الحنفي على وجوب المضمضة والاستنشاق في غسل الجنابة، بأنه عضو يجب تطهيره من النجاسة، فوجب غسله من الجنابة كسائر الأعضاء، فيقول له المالكي أو الشافعي : هذا ينتقض بداخل العينين، فإنه يجب من النجاسة ولا يجب من الجنابة، فيقول له الحنفي مجيبا : لا يلزمني هذا، لأن داخل العينين غسله عندي لا يجب غسله من النجاسة، فلا يلزمني النقض فهذا الجواب منع للنقض، ولا يجوز للمعترض أن يستدل على أن العينين يجب غسلهما من النجاسة ليلزم النقض، ولكنه يجوز له أن يقيس أن النقض يلزمه بأن يقول : لا خلاف بيني وبينك أنه لو اكتحل بالبول لوجب عليه تطهيره من النجاسة، إما بغسل وإما بمسح وإنما الخلاف بيننا في صفة التطهير الثاني : أن لا يكون للمستدل في المسألة مذهب مسطور : فهنا يمكن أن يدفع النقض بما طريقه الشرع، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن الأجرة لا تجب بعقد الإجارة، بأنه عقد على المنفعة، فلا يجب تعجيل العوض بنفس العقد كالمضاربة، فيقول الشافعي : هذا ينتقض بالنكاح، فيقول المستدل : النكاح ليس بعقد على منفعة، وإنما هو عقد على الحل والإباحة ولكي يلزم النقض لابد للمعترض أن يثبت - إن استطاع - أن النكاح عقد على منفعة، وإلا فإن النقض لا يكون لازما للمستدل ويمكن أن يدفع النقض بما طريقه العرف والعادة، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن الدنانير والدراهم لا تتعين بالعقد، بأنها لا تختلف الأغراض في أعيانها فلم تتعين بالعقد كالمكيال والميزان ، فيقول الشافعي : إنها تنتقض بالصنج، فإنها لا تختلف الأغراض في أعيانها، ومع ذلك فإنها تتعين بالعقد، فيقول المالكي بل الصنج تختلف الأغراض في أعيانها، لأن منها ما يكون من النحاس، ومنها ما يكون من الحديد ومنها ما يكون من الرصاص والحجارة، وتختلف أثمانها والأغراض فيها باختلاف أجناسها، وإنما لا تختلف الأغراض في الوزن بها، فلم يتعين الوزن بها بالعقد، وكذلك الدنانير والدراهم
The second answer: Refuting the objection by denying the cause. It is also of two types: First: that the arguer has a well-known position regarding it, such as when the Hanafi argues for the obligation of rinsing the mouth and nose in the bath of janabah, saying it is a part that must be purified from impurity, so it must be washed from janabah like other parts. The Maliki or Shafi'i says to him: This is refuted by the inside of the eyes, for it is obligatory from impurity but not from janabah. The Hanafi replies: This does not necessitate me, because the inside of the eyes, according to me, its washing is not obligatory from impurity, so the refutation is not binding. So this answer is a prevention of the refutation. It is not permissible for the objector to argue that the eyes must be washed from impurity to make the refutation binding, but it is permissible for him to analogize that the refutation is binding by saying: There is no disagreement between me and you that if one applied kohl with urine, purification from impurity would be obligatory upon him, either by washing or wiping. The disagreement is only about the manner of purification. Second: that the arguer does not have a well-known position regarding the issue. Here the refutation can be prevented by what is revealed by the Law, such as when the Maliki argues that the wage is not obligatory upon the contract of hire, because it is a contract for usufruct, so the immediate payment of the consideration is not obligatory by the contract itself, like in a mudaraba. The Shafi'i says: This is refuted by marriage. The arguer says: Marriage is not a contract for usufruct; rather it is a contract for legal permission and permissibility. For the refutation to be binding, the objector must prove, if he can, that marriage is a contract for usufruct; otherwise the refutation is not binding on the arguer. And the refutation can be prevented by what is revealed by custom and habit, such as when the Maliki argues that dinars and dirhams are not determined by the contract, because purposes do not differ in their species, so they are not determined by the contract like the measuring cup and scale. The Shafi'i says: They are refuted by weights, for purposes do not differ in their species, yet they are determined by the contract. The Maliki says: Rather, weights differ in purposes in their species, because some are copper, some iron, some lead and stone, and their prices and purposes differ according to their types. Purposes do not differ only in weighing with them, so weighing is not determined by the contract, and likewise dinars and dirhams.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the second type of response to an objection in dialectic: refuting the objection by denying the cause ('illa). The author first states that this response itself has two subdivisions. The first is when the arguer has a recorded position (madhhab masṭūr) on the point. The example given is the Hanafi arguing that rinsing the mouth and nose in the major ritual bath is obligatory because it is a part of the body that must be purified from impurity, just like other parts. The objector points to the inside of the eyes, which must be purified from impurity but not from janāba. The Hanafi responds by denying that the inside of the eyes must be purified from impurity according to his own school; thus the objection does not apply to him. The author notes that the objector cannot simply assert that the eyes must be purified from impurity; he may, however, attempt to prove it by analogy, but the arguer is not bound by the objection unless that is established. The second subdivision is when the arguer has no recorded position. Here the author gives two examples: one based on legal ruling (shar') and one based on custom ('urf). In the first, the Maliki argues that a wage is not immediately due upon a contract of hire because it is a contract for usufruct, like a mudaraba. The Shafi'i objects by citing marriage, which is also a contract for usufruct but requires immediate payment of the dower. The Maliki responds by denying that marriage is a contract for usufruct; it is a contract for legal permissibility. Thus the objector must first prove that marriage is a contract for usufruct for the objection to stand. In the custom-based example, the Maliki argues that dinars and dirhams are not determined by the contract because their specific forms do not affect the purpose; the Shafi'i objects with weights (sanj), which also do not differ in purpose but are determined by contract. The Maliki responds that weights do differ in purpose because they are made of different materials (copper, iron, etc.) and thus have different prices; only their use in weighing is indifferent. Therefore, the analogy fails.

Key Terms
دفع النقض
منع العلة
مذهب مسطور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He admits the objection but argues that janabah is different.
B) He denies that the inside of the eyes must be purified from impurity according to his school.
C) He analogizes the inside of the eyes to other parts that require purification.
D) He claims the objector has no recorded position on the matter.

Key Takeaway
Denying the cause (man' al-'illa) is a legitimate dialectical strategy that blocks an objection by showing the alleged cause does not apply, provided the arguer's school consistently holds that view or the objector cannot prove the contrary.

Refuting Objections by Showing the Restriction (Bayān al-Iḥtirāz)
Author explains the third method of refuting objections by showing the intended restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the technique of refuting an objection by showing the restriction (bayān al-iḥtirāz) in a dialectical exchange from Islamic jurisprudence.

Core Idea
The restriction is already there — you just have to show it.

This lesson introduces the third answer in the jadal tradition: refuting an objection by showing that the original argument contains a built-in restriction that excludes the alleged counterexample. This is a powerful tool because it requires careful reading of the opponent's own words rather than adding new conditions. We'll examine how Shafi'i jurists used this technique against Hanafi objections in examples about dower, compensation for usufruct, and amputation for theft.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim and the objection
Find the arguer's statement (mudda'i) and the objector's counterexample (naqd). In the first example, the Shafi'i claims the forcer owes a dower; the Hanafi objects with the Harbi case.
2
Locate the restriction in the wording
Look for a key term or phrase that implicitly limits the scope of the claim. In the example, the word 'wronged her' (ظلمها) carries an implicit restriction to those liable for compensation.
The restriction may be embedded in the ordinary meaning of the term.
3
Verify that the restriction excludes the objection
Check whether the objector's counterexample genuinely falls outside the intended scope based on the ordinary meaning of the words. The Harbi is not among those liable for compensation, so he is excluded.
4
Articulate the refutation
Show how the arguer can respond by clarifying the restriction. The Shafi'i says: 'Our saying “wronged her” refers to this forcer who is among those liable for compensation.'
This refutes the objection without changing the original claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب الثالث: دفع النقض ببيان الاحتراز : وهو قد يكون بالفعل ، وقد يكون بتفسير اللفظ . الأول : الدفع باللفظ : وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي في مهر المستكرهة على الزنا ظلمها بإتلاف ما يتقوم فلزمه الضمان كما لو أتلف عليها مالها، فيقول الحنفي : هذا يبطل بالحربي إذا وطئها فيقول : قولنا «ظلمها» يرجع إلى هذا المستكره الذي هو من أهل الضمان إذ لا يجوز أن يخلو قولنا «ظلمها» من فاعل معين وليس إلا هذا المستكره الذي هو من أهل الضمان فصار كأنا قلنا هذا الذي هو من أهل الضمان ظلمها ومثل أن يقول الشافعي في ضمان المنافع بالغصب، إن ما ضمن بالمسمى في العقد الصحيح جاز أن يضمن بالإتلاف بالعدوان المحض كالأعيان، فيقول الحنفي : هذا يبطل بالحربي، فإنه يضمن المنافع بالمسمى في العقد الصحيح ولا يضمن بالإتلاف، فيقول الشافعي : هذا لا يلزم لأنا لم نقل إن من ضمن بالمسمى ضمن بالإتلاف وإنما قلنا «ما ضمن بالمسمى ضمن بالإتلاف وتلك المنافع يجوز أن تضمن بالإتلاف، وهو إذا أتلفها مسلم أو ذمي فلا يلزم النقض ومثاله أيضًا أن يستدل المالكي على تكرر قطع السرقة في عين واحدة، بأنه حد يتعلق بفعل، فتكرره في عين كتكرره في عينين كحد الزنا، فيقول الحنفي
The third answer: Refuting the objection by showing the restriction. It may be by actual fact or by interpretation of wording. The first: refutation by wording, such as when the Shafi'i says concerning the dower of a woman forced into fornication: He wronged her by destroying what has value, so he owes compensation, just as if he destroyed her property. The Hanafi says: This is invalidated by the case of a Harbi man who has intercourse with her. He (Shafi'i) says: Our saying 'wronged her' refers to this one who forced her, who is among those liable for compensation, for it is not permissible that our saying 'wronged her' be without a specific agent, and it is none other than this forcer who is among those liable for compensation. So it is as if we said: 'This one, who is among those liable for compensation, wronged her.' And another example: The Shafi'i says concerning compensation for usufructs by usurpation: 'What is compensated by the named consideration in a valid contract may be compensated by destruction through pure aggression, like physical objects.' The Hanafi says: This is invalidated by the case of a Harbi, for he compensates usufructs by the named consideration in a valid contract but does not compensate by destruction. The Shafi'i says: This is not binding because we did not say that everyone who compensates by the named consideration compensates by destruction. Rather we said: 'What is compensated by the named consideration, compensates by destruction.' And those usufructs may be compensated by destruction, and if a Muslim or a dhimmī destroys them, then the refutation is not binding. Another example: The Maliki argues for repetition of amputation for theft in the same limb, because it is a penalty tied to an act, so its repetition in one limb is like its repetition in two limbs, like the penalty for fornication. The Hanafi says...
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage introduces the third type of response to an objection: refuting the objection by showing that the original statement includes a restriction (iḥtirāz) that excludes the alleged counterexample. This can be done in two ways: by actual fact (bi al-fi'l) or by interpretation of wording (bi tafsīr al-lafẓ). The author first explains the method by interpretation of wording with three examples. In the first, the Shafi'i argues that a man who forces a woman into fornication owes her a dower because he wronged her by destroying something of value. The Hanafi objects by citing a Harbi (non-Muslim enemy) who has intercourse with a Muslim woman and does not owe a dower. The Shafi'i responds by interpreting the word 'wronged her' (ẓalamahā) to refer specifically to the forcer who is liable for compensation (i.e., a Muslim or dhimmi), not a Harbi. Thus the restriction is built into the meaning of the term. In the second example, the Shafi'i argues that anything compensated by a named consideration in a valid contract may also be compensated by destruction through aggression, like physical objects. The Hanafi objects again with the Harbi, who compensates usufruct by the named consideration in a valid contract but not by destruction. The Shafi'i responds by clarifying that his statement 'what is compensated by the named consideration is compensated by destruction' applies to the types of usufructs that can be compensated by destruction (when destroyed by a Muslim or dhimmi), not to the Harbi case because the Harbi is not liable for destruction in that context. Thus the restriction is shown to be already implicit. The third example is left incomplete but introduces another scenario: the Maliki argues that having two amputations for theft on the same limb is like having them on two limbs, analogous to the penalty for fornication. The Hanafi presumably objects, but the text ends. The method shown here is that by carefully examining the wording of the original argument, the arguer can show that the objection's supposed counterexample actually falls outside the intended scope of the argument's terms.

Key Terms
بيان الاحتراز
تفسير اللفظ
حربي
