Ta'lil for Permissibility: Partial Acceptance Suffices in Jadal
Causation for permissibility: partial acceptance suffices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objection using 'al-qawl bi-mujab al-‘illa' is valid in a ta'lil for permissibility and distinguish it from cases requiring universal acceptance.

Core Idea
For permissibility, one accepted instance proves the cause; for obligation, all instances are needed.

This passage from the jadal tradition teaches a subtle rule: when a petitioner argues for permissibility based on a cause, the objector need only accept the consequence in one instance to challenge the cause. This contrasts with arguments for obligation, where a single counterexample refutes the claim. Let's examine the rule and its application in a Maliki-Hanafi/Shafi'i debate on the option period in sales.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule
In ta'lil for permissibility (jawaz), the objector's acceptance of the cause's consequence in even one instance suffices as a valid objection. This is because permissibility is established by a single permissive instance (al-tajwiz yaqtadi mawdu'an wahidan). The objector thereby shares the cause with the petitioner.
2
Identify the shared ground
Both parties agree on the cause (the annexed period), but they disagree on the ruling. The objector shows that in one case the cause yields a different ruling, thereby challenging the petitioner's extension.
3
Analyze the example
The Maliki claims that an option period longer than three days is permissible because it is annexed to the contract like a term. The Hanafi/Shafi'i objector says he accepts the cause, but only when a judge has ruled on it. Ask yourself: Does this acceptance match the petitioner's claim?
4
Evaluate the response
The Maliki responds that the objector's acceptance is not a true 'qawl bi-mujab al-‘illa' because it addresses a different ruling (prevention after judgment) versus initial permissibility. Thus the objection fails. This shows the importance of matching the ruling exactly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - 2 - تعليل للجواز : وهنا يكفي من القول بموجب العلة أن يبين المعترض القول بموجب العلة في موضع من المواضع، لأن التجويز يقتضي موضعا واحدا، ويكون المعترض قد شارل المستدك في العلة على وجه ما نصبها، مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن مدة الخيار يجوز اشتراطها في البيع أكثر من ثلاثة أيام بأن هذه مدة ملحقة بالعقد، فجاز أن يشترط منها أكثر من ثلاثة أيام كالأجل في البيع، فيقول الحنفي أو الشافعي : أقول : أقول بموجب هذه العلة، لأنه قد يجوز ذلك إذا حكم به حاكم برأه وأمضاه فإني أجيزه حينئذ ولا أرده ، والجواب أن يقول المالكي : هذا ليس بقول بموجب العلة، لأني قلت فجاز أن يشترط منها أكثر من ثلاثة أيام، وأنت لا تجيز بوجه ابتداء، وإنما تمنع من فسخه إذا حكم به حاكم، والامتناع من حكم آخر غير الحكم الذي عللت له من الجواز
A-2: Causation for permissibility: Here it suffices from the acceptance of the consequence of the cause that the objector shows the acceptance of the consequence of the cause in one place, because permissibility requires one place. And the objector has shared with the petitioner in the cause in the manner he established it. An example: that the Maliki argues that the option period may be stipulated in a sale for more than three days, because this period is annexed to the contract, so it is permissible to stipulate more than three days like the term in sale. Then the Hanafi or Shafi'i says: I say, I accept the consequence of this cause, because it may be permissible if a judge rules on it and upholds it, for I would permit it then and not reject it. The response is that the Maliki says: This is not an acceptance of the consequence of the cause, because I said it is permissible to stipulate more than three days, but you do not permit it initially at all; rather, you prevent its rescission if a judge rules on it. Prevention is a different ruling from the permissibility I argued for.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage explains the second sub-type of ta'lil for proving one's own madhhab: ta'lil for permissibility (jawaz). The author states the rule: in this type, the objector's acceptance of the consequence of the cause in even one instance is sufficient to constitute a valid objection. This is because permissibility is established by a single instance, unlike obligation or negation which require universality. The objector thus shares the cause with the petitioner in the manner the petitioner established it. The example given is from the Maliki school concerning the option period in a sale. The Maliki argues that an option period longer than three days is permissible because it is analogous to a term (ajal) in a sale. The Hanafi or Shafi'i objector responds that he accepts this cause, but only in the case where a judge has already ruled on it and upheld it; only then would he permit it. The Maliki's response is that this is not a true acceptance of the consequence because he (the Maliki) argued for initial permissibility (ibƟda'an), whereas the objector's acceptance is limited to a later stage after a judge's ruling, which is a different ruling. Thus the objection does not successfully refute the Maliki's claim.

Ta'lil for Invalidating Opponent's Madhhab: The Hajj Example
Author gives example of hajj to illustrate ta'lil used to invalidate opponent's madhhab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dialectical technique of ta'lil and construct a rejoinder when an opponent accepts your cause but adds a condition.

Core Idea
Accepting the cause does not mean defeat; adding a condition shifts the ruling.

In this passage, the author illustrates a key dialectical strategy: when your opponent accepts your cause but adds a condition, you can still defend your position by showing that the condition is already implicit in the cause. The example of hajj demonstrates this move, where the Hanafi argues that hajj is not obligatory by offering obedience, and the objector responds by adding 'offering capability' as a condition. This lesson walks you through the exchange to understand the logic of ta'lil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's original argument
Find the Hanafi's argument: Hajj is a worship, so it is not obligatory by mere offering of obedience (badhl al-ta'a), analogous to prayer.
The claimant uses an analogy with prayer to establish the general rule.
2
Identify the objector's response
The objector (mu'tarid) accepts the consequence of the cause (hajj is not obligatory by offering obedience) but adds a new condition: hajj is obligatory by offering capability (istita'a). He argues that if a person knows he will obey when commanded, hajj becomes obligatory even if obedience was not offered.
This is a classic ta'lil move: accepting the cause but reinterpreting its application.
3
Analyze the objector's addition
Is 'offering capability' truly a new condition, or is it already included in the original cause? The objector introduces it to salvage his madhhab without directly opposing the claimant's cause.
This step requires careful reading of the objector's reasoning: he uses the scenario of known obedience to illustrate that capability, not offering obedience, triggers obligation.
4
Examine the petitioner's rejoinders
The petitioner (claimant) offers several responses: (1) His question was about offering obedience; knowing that the person will obey constitutes offering obedience. (2) His argument was limited to the case where obedience was offered, not prior certainty. (3) Offering obedience itself creates capability, so obligation attaches to it.
Each rejoinder aims to show that the added condition is already covered by the original cause or that the argument's scope was different.
5
Determine the outcome of the exchange
The petitioner can defend his position by showing that the objector's added condition does not actually contradict the original argument. Thus, the objector's acceptance of the consequence does not invalidate the petitioner's madhhab.
This outcome demonstrates that ta'lil is a strategic move that requires careful response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - التعليل لإبطال مذهب المخالف : وذلك مثل أن يقول الحنفي : إن الحج عبادة، فلا تجب ببذل الطاعة. كالصلاة، فيقول المعترض : أقول بموجب هذه العلة فإنها لا تجب عندي ببذل الطاعة، وإنما تجب ببذل الاستطاعة لأنه لو علم أنه إذا أمره أطاع لزمه الحج، وإن كان لم يبذل له الطاعة فيجيب المستدل قائلا : إنما سألتني : هل يجب الحج ببذل الطاعة؟ ولأنه إذا علم أنه يطيعه إذا أمره، فقد علم أنه باذل للطاعة، كما يمكنه أن يجيب قائلا : إنما دللت في الموضع الذي بذل في الطاعة، ولم يكن واثقا ببذله قبل ذلك، كما قد يجيب قائلا : إنما صار مستطيعا ببذل الطاعة، فبها تعلق الوجوب
B - Causation to invalidate the opponent's madhhab: That is like when the Hanafi says: Hajj is a worship, so it is not obligatory by offering obedience, like prayer. Then the objector says: I accept the consequence of this cause, for it is not obligatory in my view by offering obedience, but it is obligatory by offering capability because if he knows that if he commands him he obeys, hajj becomes obligatory upon him, even if obedience was not offered to him. Then the petitioner responds saying: I asked you: Is hajj obligatory by offering obedience? Because if he knows that he obeys him if he commands him, then he knows that he is offering obedience. He can also respond saying: I only argued in the situation where obedience was offered, and I was not confident of his offering before that. He may also respond saying: He only becomes capable by offering obedience, so obligation attaches to it.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage explains the second main type of ta'lil (causation) in dialectic: that which aims to invalidate the opponent's madhhab. The author provides an example from the debate on hajj. The Hanafi argues that hajj, being a worship, is not obligatory by merely offering obedience (i.e., offering to perform it), analogous to prayer. The objector (likely a Shafi'i or Maliki) responds by accepting the consequence of the cause: he agrees that hajj is not obligatory by offering obedience, but he adds that it is obligatory by offering capability (istita'a). This is a reinterpretation of the cause to salvage the opponent's position. The petitioner then offers several possible responses: (1) He can argue that his question was precisely whether it is obligatory by offering obedience, and if the person knows he will obey, that itself constitutes an offering of obedience. (2) He can say that his argument was specifically about the case where obedience was offered, not about prior certainty. (3) He can say that offering obedience itself creates capability, so the obligation attaches to that. Thus, the objector's acceptance of the consequence does not invalidate the petitioner's argument because the objector is adding a condition that was not part of the original cause.

بذل الطاعة
بذل الاستطاعة

Key Takeaway
The dialectical technique of ta'lil allows a party to accept the opponent's cause while adding a condition that preserves his own madhhab.

Two Types of 'Qawl bi-Mujab al-Illa' and Three Rejoinders
Author explains two types of objection by accepting the opponent's cause and three rejoinders.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of 'al-qawl bi-mujab al-illa' (reasoning for permissibility and reasoning to invalidate) and apply the three rejoinders in a usuli debate.

Core Idea
Accept your opponent's cause but redirect its implication.

In dialectical reasoning (jadal), a powerful objection is to accept the opponent's cause (illa) but show that its logical implication leads to a different conclusion than intended. This lesson unpacks two types of this technique—'al-ta'lil li'l-jawaz' and 'al-ta'lil li-ibtal madhhab al-mukhalif'—and the three responses the original arguer can make. Through concrete examples from Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i debates, you will learn to wield and counter this subtle move.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of objection
In type A (al-ta'lil li'l-jawaz), the objector accepts the cause but limits it to a narrow permissible case, not the opponent's general claim. In type B (al-ta'lil li-ibtal madhhab al-mukhalif), the objector accepts the cause but shows it leads to a conclusion that contradicts the opponent's own school.
Type B is often more devastating because it reveals an internal inconsistency.
2
Trace the objector's logical move
The objector does not reject the cause; he accepts it and then claims the consequence supports a different ruling. Identify where the objector says 'I say by the implication of this cause' (أقول بموجب هذه العلة).
3
Examine the three rejoinders from the arguer
The arguer can (1) accuse the objector of retracting his original question, (2) reinterpret the cause's implication to show it still supports the original claim, or (3) test the objection by showing its consequence leads to an absurdity in another case.
These rejoinders are demonstrated in the ijara example.
4
Apply to the ijara example
The Maliki/Shafi'i argues lease is not dissolved by death. The Hanafi objects accepting the cause but says transfer of ownership is the true invalidator. The Maliki/Shafi'i uses all three rejoinders: (1) you asked about death, so retraction is not allowed; (2) death is the cause of transfer, so it still invalidates; (3) if transfer were the invalidator, emancipation would also dissolve lease, but it does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

العلة، لأني قلت فجاز أن يشترط منها أكثر من ثلاثة أيام، وأنت لا تجيز بوجه ابتداء، وإنما تمنع من فسخه إذا حكم به حاكم، والامتناع من حكم آخر غير الحكم الذي عللت له من الجواز. ب - التعليل لإبطال مذهب المخالف : وذلك مثل أن يقول الحنفي : إن الحج عبادة، فلا تجب ببذل الطاعة. كالصلاة، فيقول المعترض : أقول بموجب هذه العلة فإنها لا تجب عندي ببذل الطاعة، وإنما تجب ببذل الاستطاعة لأنه لو علم أنه إذا أمره أطاع لزمه الحج، وإن كان لم يبذل له الطاعة فيجيب المستدل قائلا : إنما سألتني : هل يجب الحج ببذل الطاعة؟ ولأنه إذا علم أنه يطيعه إذا أمره، فقد علم أنه باذل للطاعة، كما يمكنه أن يجيب قائلا : إنما دللت في الموضع الذي بذل في الطاعة، ولم يكن واثقا ببذله قبل ذلك، كما قد يجيب قائلا : إنما صار مستطيعا ببذل الطاعة، فبها تعلق الوجوب. ومثاله أيضًا أن يقول المالكي أو الشافعي في الإجارة: إنها لا تنفسخ بالموت لأن الموت معنى يزيل التكليف، فلا تبطل الإجارة مع سلامة المعقود عليه، كالجنون أو الإغماء، فيقول الحنفي معترضا : أقول بموجب هذه العلة، لأن الذي يزيل التكليف هو الموت والموت عندي لا يبطل الإجارة، وإنما يبطلها انتقال الملك، فلهذا نقول : إذا كان المؤجر وصيّا في حق اليتيم فمات لم تنفسخ الإجارة بموته حين لم ينتقل الملك بموته، فلو انتقل بغير موت بأن باع المستأجر منه بإذن المستأجر، بطلت الإجارة وإن لم يوجد الموت المزيل للتكليف، فدل على أن المبطل عندنا ما قلناه. هذا والجواب عن من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : أن يقول المستدل : هذا رجوع عما سألت لأنك سألتني عن الإجارة هل تنفسخ بالموت، فلما أجبت أنها لا تنفسخ بالموت، طالبتني بالدلالة عليه، فدل على أنها عندك إنما تنفسخ بالموت، وقد دَلَّلْتَ على أن الموت لا يجوز أن يبطل الإجارة، فلا يقبل رجوعك بعد التسليم. والثاني هو أنه أن يقول : معنى قولي يزيل التكليف فلا تبطل الإجارة»، يقتضي لا يبطل الإجارة ولا يكون سببًا في إبطالها، وعندك وإن كان المبطل هو الملك، إلا أن الموت هو سبب في ذلك، لأن بوجوده ينتقل الملك إليه. والثالث: أن يدل على أنه لا يجوز أن يبطل أيضًا بانتقال الملك، لأنه لو كان ذلك يبطل الإجارة لوجب إذا أجر عبده ثم أعتقه أن تنفسخ الإجارة، لأن الملك فيه قد انتقل، ولما لم تبطل دل على أن انتقال الملك لا يوجب الفسخ. أمثلة هذا النوع أيضًا أن يقول الشافعي في القاتل الملتجئ إلى الحرم، وجد سبب جواز استيفاء القصاص، فكان جائزا ، فيقول الحنفي معترضا : أقول بموجب الدليل وأن استيفاء القصاص جائز، وإنما النزاع في جواز هتك حرمة الحرم، فيرد الشافعي مجيبا : إنما كان نزاعنا في جواز استيفاء القصاص من الملتجئ، وأنت تقول به، فلا معنى للنزاع بعد ذلك.
This objection is valid, because the cause requires that it does not purify in any case, because negation is general. a - Reasoning for permissibility: Here it suffices from saying by the implication of the cause that the objector show saying by the implication of the cause in one place among places, because authorization requires a single place, and the objector would have shared with the arguer in the cause in the way he set it up, like when the Maliki argues that the period of option (khiyar) may be stipulated in sale for more than three days, because this period is annexed to the contract, so it is permissible to stipulate more than three days like the term in sale. Then the Hanafi or Shafi'i says: I say by the implication of this cause, because it may be permitted if a judge rules on it and ratifies it, so I permit it then and do not reject it. The answer is that the Maliki says: This is not saying by the implication of the cause, because I said it is permissible to stipulate more than three days, and you do not permit it in any way initially, but only prevent its annulment if a judge rules on it, and prevention is a different ruling from the one you reasoned for, which is permissibility. b - Reasoning to invalidate the opponent's view: Such as when the Hanafi says: Hajj is an act of worship, so it is not obligated by offering obedience, like prayer. Then the objector says: I say by the implication of this cause, for it is not obligated by me by offering obedience, but rather it is obligated by offering ability (istita'a), because if it were known that if he commands him he obeys, Hajj would be incumbent upon him, even if he had not offered him obedience. Then the arguer responds saying: You asked me: Is Hajj obligated by offering obedience? And because if he knows that he will obey him if he commands him, he has known that he is offering obedience. Alternatively he may respond saying: I only indicated in the situation where obedience has been offered, and he was not confident of its offer before that. Also he may respond saying: He only becomes capable (mustati') by offering obedience, so obligation is attached to it. Another example: The Maliki or Shafi'i says regarding lease (ijara): It is not dissolved by death because death is a meaning that removes obligation, so lease is not invalidated with the safety of the contracted object, like insanity or unconsciousness. Then the Hanafi objects: I say by the implication of this cause, because that which removes obligation is death, and death in my view does not invalidate lease, but rather transfer of ownership invalidates it. Hence we say: If the lessor is a guardian regarding the orphan and dies, lease is not dissolved by his death since ownership does not transfer by his death. If it transfers without death, such as if the lessee sells from him with the lessee's permission, lease is invalid even if death, which removes obligation, is not present. So it indicates that the invalidating factor in our view is what we said. And the answer to this has three aspects: The first: That the arguer says: This is a retraction from what you asked, because you asked me about lease: Is it dissolved by death? When I answered that it is not dissolved by death, you demanded evidence for it. So it indicates that in your view it is dissolved by death, and you have argued that death cannot invalidate lease, so your retraction after acceptance is not admissible. The second: That he says: The meaning of my saying 'removes obligation so lease is not invalidated' implies that death does not invalidate lease nor is it a cause for its invalidation, and in your view, even if the invalidator is ownership, death is a cause for that, because by its existence ownership transfers to him. The third: That he demonstrates that it is not permissible to invalidate by transfer of ownership either, because if that were to invalidate lease, then if he leased his slave and then emancipated him, lease should be dissolved because ownership has transferred. Since it is not dissolved, it indicates that transfer of ownership does not necessitate dissolution. Examples of this type also: The Shafi'i says regarding the killer who takes refuge in the Haram: The cause for allowing retribution exists, so it is permissible. Then the Hanafi objects: I say by the implication of the evidence, and that retribution is permissible, but the dispute is about the permissibility of violating the sanctity of the Haram. Then the Shafi'i responds: Our dispute was about the permissibility of carrying out retribution from the one who takes refuge, and you affirm it, so there is no point in disputing after that.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical (jadal) exposition explaining two types of the objection known as 'al-qawl bi-mujab al-illa' (saying by the implication of the cause) and then three possible rejoinders from the original arguer. The author first states that the objection is valid because the cause demands a general negation. Then the first type (أ) is 'ta'lil li'l-jawaz' (reasoning for permissibility). Here, the objector accepts the opponent's cause but applies it to a single case where the ruling holds, thus neutralizing the argument. The example involves the Maliki arguing that a khiyar period longer than three days is permissible because it is annexed to the contract like a term. The Hanafi/Shafi'i objector says he accepts the implication but only when a judge has ruled on it. The Maliki responds that this is not a proper 'qawl bi-mujab' because he argued for initial permissibility, not a later ruling. The second type (ب) is 'ta'lil li-ibtal madhhab al-mukhalif' (reasoning to invalidate the opponent's view). Here, the objector accepts the cause but shows that it leads to a different conclusion than the one the arguer intended. The example is the Hanafi arguing that Hajj is not obligated by offering obedience because it is an act of worship like prayer. The objector (e.g., a Maliki) says he accepts that Hajj is not obligated by offering obedience, but it is obligated by offering ability (istita'a). The Hanafi then has several possible responses: he can argue that the question was specifically about offering obedience, or that offering ability presupposes offering obedience. Another example involves lease (ijara): the Maliki/Shafi'i argues lease is not dissolved by death because death removes obligation, like insanity. The Hanafi objector says he accepts that death does not dissolve lease, but the actual invalidator is transfer of ownership. He then gives a scenario to support this. The author then presents three possible answers (وجوه) from the original arguer to this objection. The first: the objector has contradicted himself by first accepting that death does not dissolve lease but then demanding evidence for the opposite. The second: the arguer's statement 'death removes obligation so lease is not invalidated' means death is not a direct invalidator, but the objector's own view treats death as a cause for transfer of ownership. The third: demonstrating that transfer of ownership cannot be the invalidator either, because if it were, emancipating a slave would dissolve the lease, which it does not. Finally, another example is given of the second type: the Shafi'i argues that retribution is permissible for a killer in the Haram because the cause exists. The Hanafi objector accepts that retribution is permissible but disputes the violation of the Haram's sanctity. The Shafi'i responds that the dispute was about retribution, not sanctity, and since the objector agrees on retribution, the dispute is resolved. This passage illustrates the nuanced dialectical technique of accepting an opponent's premise but drawing a different conclusion, and the strategies to counter such objections.

Key Terms
القول بموجب العلة
التعليل للجواز
التعليل لإبطال مذهب المخالف
العلة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the third response (الجواب الثالث) of the arguer to the objection that lease (ijara) is dissolved by transfer of ownership rather than death?
A) That the objector has retracted his question.
B) That lease would also be dissolved by emancipation of a slave, which it is not, proving transfer of ownership is not the invalidator.
C) That death is not a cause for dissolution because obligation continues after death.
D) That the objector's example of the guardian is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The technique of 'qawl bi-mujab al-illa' turns the opponent's own reasoning against them by showing it supports a different ruling.

Suppose the Shafi'i argues that istishab is valid because it preserves the status quo. Dialectical Exchange on Lease Dissolution by Death
Author presents a jadal exchange between Hanafi and Shafi'i on whether death dissolves a lease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the structure of a jadal exchange: identify the initial claim, the objection (i'tirad), and the responses (jawab), and evaluate the effectiveness of each response.

Core Idea
A jadal objection often concedes the cause but reinterprets its application.

In Islamic legal dialectic, a common move is to accept the opponent's 'illa but argue that it does not lead to the claimed ruling. This passage shows how a Hanafi objects to a Shafi'i's argument about lease dissolution by death, and how the Shafi'i responds with three distinct strategies. Understanding this exchange sharpens your ability to follow and construct valid objections and responses in munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i's initial argument
Find the Shafi'i's claim: death does not dissolve a lease because death removes obligation but the contracted object remains intact, analogous to insanity or fainting.
The analogy is from usul al-fiqh: qiyas.
2
Locate the Hanafi's objection
The Hanafi says 'I affirm by virtue of this cause' (a common jadal move), but then argues that the real invalidator is transfer of ownership, not death. He provides evidence from a guardian scenario.
This is a mu'tarid (objector) accepting the cause but redefining its scope.
3
Extract the three Shafi'i responses
First: accuses the Hanafi of retracting from his original question. Second: refines the cause statement to mean death is not a cause of dissolution but is a cause of ownership transfer. Third: gives a counterexample (emancipation of a rented slave) to show ownership transfer alone does not dissolve the lease.
Each response is a different jawab strategy.
4
Evaluate which response is strongest
Consider whether each answer successfully counters the objection. The first response is a procedural objection (retraction), the second clarifies the cause, the third offers a counterargument.
Assess the logical force of each based on jadal rules.
5
Apply the same structure to the second example
In the killer seeking refuge example, the Shafi'i argues execution is permissible because the cause exists; the Hanafi objects that the dispute is about violating the sanctity of the sanctuary; the Shafi'i responds that the dispute was about execution permissibility, which is conceded.
This shows the same pattern: accepting the cause but reinterpreting the point of disagreement.

بالموت لأن الموت معنى يزيل التكليف، فلا تبطل الإجارة مع سلامة المعقود عليه، كالجنون أو الإغماء، فيقول الحنفي معترضا : أقول بموجب هذه العلة، لأن الذي يزيل التكليف هو الموت والموت عندي لا يبطل الإجارة، وإنما يبطلها انتقال الملك، فلهذا نقول : إذا كان المؤجر وصيّا في حق اليتيم فمات لم تنفسخ الإجارة بموته حين لم ينتقل الملك بموته، فلو انتقل بغير موت بأن باع المستأجر منه بإذن المستأجر، بطلت الإجارة وإن لم يوجد الموت المزيل للتكليف، فدل على أن المبطل عندنا ما قلناه . هذا والجواب عن من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : أن يقول المستدل : هذا رجوع عما سألت لأنك سألتني عن الإجارة هل تنفسخ بالموت، فلما أجبت أنها لا تنفسخ بالموت، طالبتني بالدلالة عليه، فدل على أنها عندك إنما تنفسخ بالموت، وقد دَلَّلْتَ على أن الموت لا يجوز أن يبطل الإجارة، فلا يقبل رجوعك بعد التسليم. والثاني هو أنه أن يقول : معنى قولي يزيل التكليف فلا تبطل الإجارة»، يقتضي لا يبطل الإجارة ولا يكون سببًا في إبطالها، وعندك وإن كان المبطل هو الملك، إلا أن الموت هو سبب في ذلك، لأن بوجوده ينتقل الملك إليه. والثالث: أن يدل على أنه لا يجوز أن يبطل أيضًا بانتقال الملك، لأنه لو كان ذلك يبطل الإجارة لوجب إذا أجر عبده ثم أعتقه أن تنفسخ الإجارة، لأن الملك فيه قد انتقل، ولما لم تبطل دل على أن انتقال الملك لا يوجب الفسخ أمثلة هذا النوع أيضًا أن يقول الشافعي في القاتل الملتجئ إلى الحرم، وجد سبب جواز استيفاء القصاص، فكان جائزا ، فيقول الحنفي معترضا : أقول بموجب الدليل وأن استيفاء القصاص جائز، وإنما النزاع في جواز هتك حرمة الحرم، فيرد الشافعي مجيبا : إنما كان نزاعنا في جواز استيفاء القصاص من الملتجئ، وأنت تقول به، فلا معنى للنزاع بعد ذلك
Because death is a meaning that removes obligation, so the lease is not invalidated with the safety of the contracted object, like insanity or fainting. So the Hanafi says objecting: I affirm by virtue of this cause, because that which removes obligation is death, and death in my view does not invalidate the lease; rather, it is invalidated by the transfer of ownership. For this we say: If the lessor was a guardian over an orphan and died, the lease is not dissolved by his death when ownership does not transfer by his death. But if it transfers without death, such as if the lessor sold to the lessee with the lessee's permission, the lease is invalidated even though the death that removes obligation did not occur. So it indicates that the invalidator in our view is what we said. This, and the answer to this is from three aspects: First: The proponent should say: This is a retraction from what you asked, because you asked me about the lease: is it dissolved by death? When I answered that it is not dissolved by death, you demanded evidence for it. So it indicates that in your view it is dissolved by death, and you have argued that death cannot invalidate the lease. So your retraction after concession is not accepted. Second: He should say: The meaning of my statement 'removes obligation so the lease is not invalidated' implies that death does not invalidate the lease and is not a cause for its invalidation. In your view, even if the invalidator is ownership, death is a cause in that because by its occurrence ownership transfers to him. Third: He should argue that it is also not permissible to be invalidated by transfer of ownership, because if that invalidated the lease, then if a man rented his slave then emancipated him, the lease should be dissolved because ownership in him had transferred. But since it was not invalidated, it indicates that transfer of ownership does not necessitate dissolution. Examples of this type also: The Shafi'i says concerning the killer who seeks refuge in the sanctuary: 'The cause for the permissibility of executing retribution existed, so it is permissible.' So the Hanafi says objecting: 'I affirm by virtue of the evidence that execution of retribution is permissible; the dispute is only about the permissibility of violating the sanctity of the sanctuary.' So the Shafi'i replies: 'Our dispute was only about the permissibility of executing retribution from the one seeking refuge, and you affirm it, so there is no point to dispute after that.'
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange (jadal) between a Hanafi and a Shafi'i concerning whether death dissolves a lease (ijara). The Shafi'i initially argues that death should not dissolve a lease because death removes legal obligation (taklif) but the contracted object remains intact, analogizing death to insanity or fainting. The Hanafi objects by saying he agrees with the cause (that death removes obligation) but argues that the actual invalidator is transfer of ownership, not death itself. He provides evidence: if the lessor is a guardian over an orphan, his death does not transfer ownership to the lessee (since the orphan inherits), so the lease remains; but if ownership transfers without death (e.g., the lessor sells to the lessee with permission), the lease is invalidated. Thus, the invalidator is transfer of ownership. The author then gives three possible answers (jawab) from the Shafi'i (the original proponent) to counter the Hanafi's objection. First, he accuses the Hanafi of retracting: the Hanafi initially asked whether death dissolves the lease, but now argues that death is irrelevant, which is a contradiction. Second, he refines his own statement: death removes obligation and is not a cause for invalidation, but the Hanafi admits that death causes transfer of ownership, so indirectly death becomes a cause. Third, he argues that if transfer of ownership were the invalidator, then emancipating a rented slave would dissolve the lease, but it does not, showing transfer of ownership alone is not sufficient. The passage then gives another example: a killer seeking refuge in the sanctuary; the Shafi'i says the cause for executing retribution exists, so it is permissible. The Hanafi objects that the dispute is about violating sanctuary sanctity, not about retribution. The Shafi'i replies that the dispute was about executing retribution from the sanctuary, and the Hanafi agrees it is permissible, so there is no dispute left.

Key Terms
علة
اعتراض
جواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dialectical example about lease dissolution by death, what is the Hanafi's main argument?
A) Death does not remove obligation, so the lease should dissolve.
B) Death does not invalidate the lease; rather, transfer of ownership does.
C) The lease is invalidated by death because the leased object is destroyed.
D) The Shafi'i's cause is invalid because death is not an effective cause.

Key Takeaway
A jadal objection that says 'I affirm by virtue of this cause' is not a concession but a strategic move to shift the 'illa, requiring multiple response strategies from the proponent.

Construct an objection to the following Shafi'i argument: 'The sale of an unborn animal (habal al-habala) is invalid because it is a sale of gharar (uncertainty).' Use the Hanafi pattern: 'I affirm by virtue of this cause, but...' and provide a counterexample.

The Definition and Authority of Ijma' in Dialectic
Definition of ijma' and the first objection to its authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the jadal exchange regarding the authority of ijma' and identify the initial objection and its rebuttal.

Core Idea
Ijma' authority was contested: how does one defend it against the charge that it is not a proof?

The passage begins by defining ijma' and its binding nature, then immediately notes that this was not universally accepted. The first specific objection is that ijma' cannot be used as evidence. The response appeals to the widespread acceptance among the majority of scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of ijma' and its claim to be a proof.
Find where the author defines ijma' as agreement of mujtahids after the Prophet's death on a legal ruling, and states it is a binding proof.
2
Locate the objection.
Find the part where the objector denies that ijma' can be used as a proof in legal rulings, claiming it is not suitable for deriving rulings.
3
Find the rebuttal.
The author counters by citing that the majority of legal theorists (most Muslims, per al-Amidi) accept ijma' as a legal proof, dismissing the objection as deviant.
Notice this is a meta-argument: using consensus to defend consensus.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإجماع - كما هو معلوم - اتفاق مجتهدي أمة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم بعد وفاته في من العصور على حكم شرعي في حادثة (1) وهو دليل شرعي، فإذا اتفق المجتهدون على حكم ما في مسألة معينة كان اتفاقهم ذاك ملزما لبقية المسلمين في الأخذ به، ولا يجوز العدول عنه إلى اتباع الهوى أو الرأي المستند إلى دليل أقل قيمة من دليل الإجماع، قال الإمام أبي إسحاق الشيرازي: «وهـو – أي الإجماع - حجة من حجج الشرع، ودليل من أدلة الأحكام) (2) . لكن مع ذلك، فإن القول بحجية الإجماع لم تكن محل تسليم مطلق، كما أن الاستدلال بالإجماع في جزئيات الأحكام لم يكن يسلم دائما من النقد، أو يُسلّم به لأول وهلة، بل لقد وجهت إلى مفهوم الإجماع بعض الانتقاضات، كما رأى البعض عدم اعتباره، وحتى الذين يقولون به كانوا أحيانًا يعدلون عن الأخذ به إذا لم يثبت، أو كان مخالفًا لدليل أقوى منه في نظرهم، إلى غير ذلك . وهو ما يعني أن الإجماع أيضًا كان الاستدلال به مجالا للجدل والنزاع بين الأصوليين، حيث يثور النزاع في حكم مسألة جزئية معينة، فإذا ما استدل أحد المخالفين بدليل الإجماع لرأيه في المسألة، انتقل الخلاف إلى المسألة إلى جدل في المكانة الأصولية للدليل المستدل به وهو الإجماع. وقد كانت الاعتراضات التي وردت في هذا الشأن الجدل حول الإجماع كما يلي :

المطلب الأول : الاعتراضات من جهة الحجية
أي أن يستدل مُدَّعُ بالإجماع في حكم مسألة من المسائل، فيواجهه المعترض بأن الإجماع لا يحتج به في الأحكام الشرعية، ولا يصلح دليلا تستنبط به الأحكام. والواقع أن حجية الإجماع هي محل اتفاق بين جمهور الأصوليين ولم يخالف في ذلك إلا شواذ لا اعتبار لرأيهم ولا سند له. قال الإمام سيف الدين الآمدي رحمه الله :
اتفق أكثر المسلمين على أن الإجماع حجة شرعية يجب العمل به على كل مسلم خلافًا للشيعة، والخوارج، والنظام من المعتزلة» (1) . وقد استدل علماء الأصول للقول بحجية الإجماع بأدلة كثيرة، وهذه الأدلة أورد عليها المخالفون جملة من الاعتراضات التي لم ترق إلى درجة رد تلك الأدلة أو منع الاحتجاج بها، وهذه الأدلة والاعتراضات مفصلة في كتب أصول الفقه، ونكتفي هنا بإيراد دليل واحد منها مقرونا بالاعتراضات عليه والردود على تلك الاعتراضات .
فقد استدل جمهور الأصوليين للقول بحجية الإجماع من القرآن بقوله تعالى: وَمَن يُشَاقِقِ الرَّسُولَ مِنْ بَعْدِ مَا نَبَيَّنَ لَهُ الْهُدَى وَيَتَّبِعْ غَيْرَ سَبِيلِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ نُوَلِهِ مَا تَوَلَّى وَنُصْلِهِ جَهَنَّمَ وَسَاءَتْ مَصِيرًا (2)
ووجه الاحتجاج بالآية، أن الله تعالى جمع بين مشاقة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم واتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين في الوعيد، حيث قال تعالى : نُوَلِهِ مَا تَوَلَّى وَنُصْلِهِ جَهَنَّمَ ... ، فيلزم من ذلك أن يكون اتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين محرما، لأنه لو لم يكن حرامًا لما جمع بينه وبين المحرم الذي هو المشاقة في الوعيد، فإنه لا يحسن الجمع بين حرام وحلال في وعيد وإذا حرم اتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين وجب اتباع سبيلهم، لأنه لا مخرج عنهما، ويلزم من وجوب اتباع سبيل المؤمنين
Consensus - as is known - is the agreement of the mujtahids of the nation of Muhammad, peace be upon him, after his death in any age upon a legal ruling concerning an incident (1), and it is a legal proof. So if the mujtahids agree upon a ruling on a particular issue, their agreement is binding upon the rest of the Muslims to adopt it, and it is not permissible to deviate from it in favor of following desire or an opinion based on a proof of lesser value than the proof of consensus. Imam Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi said: 'It - i.e. consensus - is a proof among the proofs of the Sharia, and a evidence among the evidences of rulings' (2). But despite that, the statement about the authority of consensus was not a matter of absolute acceptance, just as argumentation by consensus in particular rulings was not always free from criticism, nor accepted at first glance. Rather, some objections were directed at the concept of consensus, just as some did not consider it, and even those who do accept it sometimes deviate from adopting it if it is not established, or if it contradicts a stronger proof in their view, etc. This means that argumentation by consensus was also a field for dialectic and dispute among the legal theorists, for a dispute arises over the ruling of a particular issue, and if one of the opponents argues by consensus for his opinion on the issue, the disagreement moves from the issue to a dialectic concerning the legal status of the proof used, which is consensus. The objections that occurred in this regard concerning dialectic about consensus are as follows:

The first demand: Objections from the aspect of authority
That a claimant argues by consensus on the ruling of an issue, and the objector faces him by saying that consensus is not used as proof in legal rulings, nor is it suitable as a proof from which rulings are derived. The reality is that the authority of consensus is a matter of agreement among the majority of legal theorists, and none opposed that except deviants whose opinion is not considered and has no support. Imam Sayf al-Din al-Amidi, may God have mercy on him, said: 'The majority of Muslims agreed that consensus is a legal proof that must be acted upon by every Muslim, contrary to the Shiites, Kharijites, and al-Nazzam of the Mu'tazila' (1). The scholars of usul have adduced many proofs for the statement about the authority of consensus, and the opponents raised a number of objections against these proofs that did not reach the level of refuting those proofs or preventing the argument by them. These proofs and objections are detailed in books of usul al-fiqh, and we suffice here to cite one proof from them, accompanied by the objections to it and the responses to those objections.
The majority of legal theorists adduced from the Quran for the authority of consensus the saying of the Most High: 'But whoever contradicts the Messenger after guidance has become clear to him and follows a path other than that of the believers - We will give him what he has taken and cause him to enter Hell, and evil is the destination' (2). The aspect of argument by the verse is that God, Most High, combined opposing the Messenger, peace be upon him, and following a path other than that of the believers in the threat, as He said: 'We will give him what he has taken and cause him to enter Hell...' So it follows from that that following a path other than that of the believers is forbidden, because if it were not forbidden, He would not have combined it with what is forbidden, which is opposition, in the threat. For it is not appropriate to combine the forbidden and the permissible in one threat. And if following a path other than that of the believers is forbidden, then following their path becomes obligatory, because there is no way out of the two. And it follows from the obligation to follow the path of the believers...
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage introduces the first major area of jadal (dialectic) in usul al-fiqh concerning the proof of ijma' (consensus). It begins by defining ijma' as the agreement of the mujtahids of the Muslim community after the Prophet's death on a legal ruling, and establishes that it is a binding legal proof. However, the author immediately notes that this binding nature was not universally accepted; there were objections, and even its proponents sometimes set it aside in the face of stronger evidence. This sets up the dialectical context: when a disputant argues a case using ijma', the opponent may challenge the very authority of ijma' as a proof, shifting the debate from the substantive issue to the epistemological status of the proof itself.

The text then presents the first specific objection: 'Objections from the aspect of authority.' The objector claims that ijma' should not be accepted as evidence in legal rulings. The response is that the authority (hujjiyya) of ijma' is agreed upon by the vast majority of legal theorists, with only negligible dissent. The author cites Imam al-Amidi to affirm that most Muslims consider ijma' a binding legal proof, except for some sects like the Shi'a, Khawarij, and al-Nazzam. This appeal to consensus on the authority of consensus is a meta-argument: the very source under attack is defended by pointing to its widespread acceptance.

Next, the author states that the proponents of ijma' have adduced numerous proofs for its authority, and although opponents raised objections, these did not refute the proofs. One such proof from the Qur'an is then provided: verse 4:115, which threatens those who oppose the Messenger and follow a path other than that of the believers. The argument (wajh al-ihtijaj) is that by combining opposition to the Messenger (which is clearly forbidden) with following a non-believers' path in a single threat, God indicates that following a different path is also forbidden. The reasoning is that it is improper to join a permissible act with a forbidden one in a threat; therefore, following a non-believers' path must be forbidden. This implies that following the believers' path (i.e., adopting the consensus of the community) is obligatory, as the only alternative to the forbidden path.

This is a typical dialectical debate in usul al-fiqh, where the authority of a source (ijma') is defended via a combined appeal to widespread acceptance and scriptural evidence. The commentary here follows the author's order: definition, problem statement, objection, rebuttal, and scriptural proof.

Key Terms
إجماع
حجية

According to the passage, what is the basis for the rebuttal to the objection that ijma' is not a proof?
A) The verse 4:115
B) The agreement of the majority of scholars
C) Logical necessity
D) The sunna of the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The debate over ijma' authority often shifts from the substantive issue to the epistemological status of the proof itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
State the objection and rebuttal in a jadal format: take the role of the mu'tarid and challenge the authority of ijma', then the mudda'i defends it using the passage's rebuttal.
?
Identify another example from usul al-fiqh where a proof's authority is contested (e.g., qiyas) and outline the jadal exchange.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
8 min
The Qur'anic Proof for Ijma' and Its Logical Structure
The Qur'anic proof for ijma' authority using verse 4:115.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical inference from Qur'an 4:115 used to prove the authority of ijma'.

Core Idea
The verse's threat structure implies that following the believers' path is obligatory.

The passage now presents one scriptural proof for ijma' authority: Qur'an 4:115. The argument is that God combines opposition to the Messenger with following a path other than the believers in a single threat, implying that the latter is also forbidden, and thus the former (following the believers) is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verse and the two actions combined in the threat.
Find the verse and note that it mentions opposing the Messenger and following a path other than the believers together in the same threat of punishment.
2
Extract the logical principle.
The author states that it is not appropriate (لا يحسن) to combine a forbidden act and a permissible act in a single threat.
3
Apply the principle.
Since opposing the Messenger is clearly forbidden, the act of following another path must also be forbidden, otherwise the combination would be improper.
4
Recognize the disjunction.
Only two paths exist: the believers' path and other paths. Forbidding one makes the other obligatory, because there is no third option (لا مخرج عنهما).
This is a disjunctive syllogism: either A or B; not B; therefore A.

Seven Dialectical Objections to the Consensus Proof and Their Rebuttals
Seven dialectical objections against the proof of consensus from the Quranic verse and their rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical exchange in usul al-fiqh by identifying the objection (i'tirad) and rebuttal (jawab) in each of the seven challenges to the proof of consensus from Quran 4:115.

Core Idea
Dialectical objections test a proof's strength; each rebuttal must be a valid response within the rules of jadal.

The Quranic verse 'And whoever opposes the Messenger... and follows other than the way of the believers' (4:115) is used by scholars as proof for the authority of consensus (ijma'). Over time, seven primary objections were raised against this interpretation. Each objection targets a different linguistic or logical aspect, and each is answered with a precise rebuttal. In this lesson, you will walk through each objection and rebuttal to understand how dialectical reasoning operates in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Locate the main claim
Identify the central thesis being defended: Quran 4:115 proves the obligation to follow the consensus of the believers.
The proof is based on the phrase 'follows other than the way of the believers' being prohibited.
2
Examine the first objection and rebuttal
Objection: The threat is only on the combination of following non-believers and opposing the Messenger. Rebuttal: The threat is on each individually, otherwise the conjunction would be meaningless.
This shows the logical principle that a conjunction can imply individual threats if separation is meaningful.
3
Examine the second objection and rebuttal
Objection: The condition of guidance applies to both parts, so following non-believers is only prohibited after all guidance is clear. Rebuttal: Guidance means fundamental guidance (tawheed and prophethood), not subsidiary rulings.
The rebuttal narrows the scope of the condition using the context of the verse.
4
Examine the third objection and rebuttal
Objection: The singular words 'other' and 'way' lack generality. Rebuttal: Singular in construct state (idafa) gives generality, confirmed by the possibility of exception.
This is a key linguistic principle: 'الاستثناء معيار العموم' (exception is the criterion of generality).
5
Examine the fourth objection and rebuttal
Objection: 'Way' (sabil) is metaphorical and better refers to the evidence for consensus. Rebuttal: 'Way' can mean consensus linguistically, as in 'Say: This is my way', and consensus is more beneficial as it guides both mujtahid and muqallid.
The rebuttal uses a Quranic example and a practical consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولها : أن الله عز وجل رتب الوعيد على اتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين ومشاقة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ،معا ، فمتابعة غير سبيل المؤمنين محرمة إذا اقترنت بمشاقة الرسول. وأجيب على هذا الاعتراض، بأن الوعيد إنما هو مرتب على كل منهما، ولو لم يكن الأمر كذلك لما فصل بينهما ، فإذا كان الفصل لغير معنى كان ذلك لغوّا، واللغو محال على الله تعالى. الثاني : حتى مع التسليم بأن الوعيد مرتب على كل منهما، لكن لا نسلم تحريم اتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين مطلقا، بل بشرط تبين الهدى، لأن تبين الهدى شرط في المعطوف عليه لقوله تعالى: ﴿مِنْ بَعْدِ مَا تَبَيَّنَ لَهُ الْهُدَىٰ﴾ [آل عمران: 86]، فيكون أيضًا شرط في المعطوف لكونه في حكمه، فتكون حرمة اتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين متوقفة على تبين جميع أنواع الهدى. وأجيب على هذا الاعتراض بأن القول بأن الشرط في المعطوف عليه شرط في المعطوف لا يضر ، فإنه لا نزاع في أن الهدى المشروط في تحريم المشاقة إنما هو دليل التوحيد والنبوة، لا أدلة الأحكام الفرعية، فيكون هذا الهدى شرطا في اتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين، وهو مسلم. الثالث : مع التسليم بأن قول الله تعالى: ﴿وَيَتَّبِعْ غَيْرَ سَبِيلِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ﴾ [النساء: 115] يوجب تحريم المخالفة، لكن لفظي (غير) و (سبيل) مفردان، والمفرد لا يوجب تحريم كل ما غاير سبيلهم، بل يصدق على صورة متفق عليها وهي صورة الكفر ونحوه مما لا خلاف فيه، فلا عموم له. والجواب : أنه وإن كان مفردا إلا أنه يقتضي العموم لأنه مضاف، ويدل عليه الاستثناء منه فيقال إلا سبيل كذا، والاستثناء معيار العموم. الرابع: لا نسلم أن السبيل هو قول أهل الإجماع، بل دليل الإجماع وبيانه أن لغة هو الطريق الذي يمشي فيه، وقد تعذرت إرادته هنا، فتعين الحمل على المجاز، وهو إما قول أهل الإجماع أو الدليل الذي لأجله أجمعوا، والثاني أَوْلى لقلة العلاقة بينه وبين الطريق، وهو كون كل واحد منهما موصلا إلى المقصد. والجواب أن السبيل أيضًا يطلق على الإجماع، لأن أهل اللغة يطلقونه على ما يختاره الإنسان لنفسه من قول أو فعل، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿قُلْ هَٰذِهِ سَبِيلِي﴾ [يوسف: 108]، وإذا كان كذلك فحمله على الإجماع أولى لعموم فائدته، فإن الإجماع يعمل به المجتهد والمقلد، وأما الدليل فلا يعمل به سوى المجتهد. الخامس : لا نسلم أنه يجب اتباع سبيل المؤمنين في كل شيء، بل في السبيل الذي صاروا به مؤمنين ويدل عليه أن الآية الكريمة نزلت في رجل ارتد. والجواب : أن هذا الاعتراض يلزم منه أن تكون مخالفة سبيل المؤمنين هي المشاقة، لأنه لا معنى لمشاقة الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام إلا ترك الإيمان، فلو حمل على هذا لزم التكرار والتكرار مما يخالف البلاغة، ولا يكون من الله عز وجل. السادس : مع التسليم بتحريم اتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين، لكن لا نسلم وجوب اتباع سبيلهم، لأن هناك اختيارًا وسطا، وهو ترك الاتباع أصلا فلا يتبع سبيل المؤمنين ولا غير سبيلهم. والجواب أن ترك الاتباع بالكلية، هو من اتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين فمن اختاره لنفسه فقد اتبع غير سبيلهم. السابع : مع التسليم بوجوب الاتباع، إلا أنه لا يجب في كل الأمور، لأنهم لو أجمعوا على فعل مباح لا يجب متابعتهم على فعله وإلا لكان المباح واجبا، وإذا لم يجب اتباعهم في الكل لم يلزم اتباعهم فيما أجمعوا عليه لجواز أن يكون المراد هو الإيمان أو غيره مما هو متفق عليه. والجواب : أن اتباع سبيل المؤمنين كاتباع الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم فاتباعهم في المباح أيضًا واجب، ومعنى وجوبه: وجوب اعتقاد إباحته وأن يفعله الفاعل على جهة الإباحة لا على جهة أخرى.
First: God Almighty made the threat conditional on following other than the way of the believers and opposing the Messenger together, so following other than the way of the believers is forbidden only if coupled with opposing the Messenger. Answer: The threat is conditional on each individually, otherwise the conjunction would be meaningless, and meaningless speech is impossible for God. Second: Even if we accept that, we do not accept absolute prohibition of following other than the way of the believers except with the condition of clear guidance, because clear guidance is a condition in the first conjunct (opposing the Messenger) in the verse after guidance has become clear, so it is also a condition in the second conjunct. Answer: The condition is guidance in tawheed and prophethood, not subsidiary rulings, so it is conceded. Third: Even if the verse implies prohibition, the words 'other' and 'way' are singular, so the singular does not imply prohibition of every different way, but applies to an agreed-upon case like disbelief. Answer: Singular due to idafa gives generality, as shown by exception. Fourth: We do not accept that 'way' means consensus; it is either the consensus or the evidence, and the second is more appropriate. Answer: 'Way' can mean consensus linguistically as in 'Say: This is my way', and consensus is more beneficial. Fifth: We do not accept that following their way is required in everything, only in the way by which they became believers; the verse was revealed about an apostate. Answer: This would make following non-believers the same as opposing the Messenger, leading to redundancy contrary to eloquence. Sixth: Even if following non-believers is forbidden, following believers is not required; one may remain neutral. Answer: Remaining neutral is itself following non-believers. Seventh: Even if following is required, it is not required in all matters; if they agree on a permissible act, following would make it obligatory. Answer: Following in permissible acts is required in the sense of believing it permissible and doing it as permissible.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (jadal) presenting seven objections against the proof of the authority of consensus (ijma') from the Quranic verse 'And whoever opposes the Messenger after guidance has become clear to him and follows other than the way of the believers' (Quran 4:115). Each objection is followed by a rebuttal. The author systematically defends the proof. The first objection argues that the threat is only on the combination of following non-believers and opposing the Messenger. The rebuttal states that the threat is on each individually because the conjunction (wa) would be meaningless otherwise. The second objection adds that the condition of guidance being clear applies to both, so prohibition depends on all guidance being clear. The rebuttal distinguishes guidance: it refers to fundamentals, not subsidiary rulings. The third objection claims the singular words lack generality. The rebuttal invokes the principle that a noun in construct state (idafa) implies generality, and exception (istithna') proves it. The fourth objection argues that the term 'way' (sabil) is metaphorical and better applied to the evidence for consensus. The rebuttal cites linguistic usage including Quranic 'Say: This is my way' to show it includes consensus, which is more beneficial because it guides both mujtahid and muqallid. The fifth objection restricts following to matters of faith. The rebuttal shows that would make the verse redundant. The sixth objection posits a middle ground of non-following. The rebuttal counters that non-following is itself following non-believers. The seventh objection limits obligation to non-mubah matters. The rebuttal clarifies that following in mubah is obligatory in the sense of believing it permissible and doing it as such. The passage demonstrates sophisticated dialectical reasoning in usul al-fiqh.

إجماع
عموم

Dialectical exchange in usul al-fiqh is a rigorous method where objections are answered using precise lexical and logical principles, not mere opinion.

The Fourth Objection: Meaning of 'Sabil' in Following Consensus
Objection that 'sabil' refers to proof of consensus, not consensus itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to deconstruct a jadal exchange in usul al-fiqh by identifying the objector's linguistic argument and the respondent's counter-argument using alternative linguistic evidence and teleological reasoning.

Core Idea
The answer defeats the objection by showing that 'sabil' linguistically includes consensus, which benefits all, not just mujtahids.

In this passage, we see a classic dialectical move: the objector (mu'tarid) tries to restrict the meaning of a key term ('sabil') to the proof of consensus, thereby excluding consensus itself. The respondent (mujib) counters by offering a broader linguistic usage and a practical argument about general benefit. Understanding this exchange trains you in the art of jadal: how to defend a position using language and utility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's position (mudda'i)
The objector asserts that 'sabil' in the command 'follow the way of the believers' does not mean consensus (ijma'), but rather the proof (dalil) that led to consensus. Note the precise claim: he rejects the direct identification of 'sabil' with the accomplished consensus.
2
Extract the objector's linguistic grounds
He argues that 'sabil' literally means 'road'. Since the literal meaning is impossible here (it is not a physical road), it must be metaphorical. Two metaphorical candidates: (1) the consensus statement itself, (2) the proof that leads to consensus. He claims the second is more appropriate because the relationship between 'proof' and 'road' is weaker (both lead to a goal), implying less metaphoricity is needed.
The logic of 'weaker relationship → more appropriate for metaphor' is counterintuitive; it may reflect a preference for a less stretched metaphor.
3
Analyze the answerer's response (jawab)
The answerer disputes the claim that 'sabil' cannot literally refer to consensus. He provides linguistic evidence: Arabic speakers use 'sabil' for any chosen path of action or speech, citing Quran 12:108 'Say: This is my way.' Thus consensus can be called a 'sabil' directly. He then adds a teleological argument: applying it to consensus is more beneficial because consensus serves both mujtahids and laypeople, whereas the proof only serves mujtahids.
The answer uses both authoritative linguistic usage (Quran) and practical utility (maslaha) to outweigh the objector's weak connection argument.
4
Determine the outcome of the exchange
By providing a direct linguistic basis for 'sabil' as consensus and a practical reason to prefer it, the answerer successfully defends the standard interpretation. The objector's attempt to limit the term to proof is rebutted.
In jadal, the answerer wins if he can show his interpretation is linguistically valid and more beneficial to the sharīʿa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السبيل الرابع: لا نسلم أن السبيل هو قول أهل الإجماع، بل دليل الإجماع وبيانه أن لغة هو الطريق الذي يمشي فيه، وقد تعذرت إرادته هنا، فتعين الحمل على المجاز، وهو إما قول أهل الإجماع أو الدليل الذي لأجله أجمعوا، والثاني أَوْلى لقلة العلاقة بينه وبين الطريق، وهو كون كل واحد منهما موصلا إلى المقصد والجواب أن السبيل أيضًا يطلق على الإجماع، لأن أهل اللغة يطلقونه على ما يختاره الإنسان لنفسه من قول أو فعل، ومنه قوله تعالى : قُلْ هَذِهِ سَبِيلِي ، وإذا كان كذلك فحمله على الإجماع أولى لعموم فائدته، فإن سبيلي الإجماع يعمل به المجتهد والمقلد، وأما الدليل فلا يعمل به سوى المجتهد.
The fourth way: We do not accept that 'the way' is the statement of the people of consensus, rather it is the proof of consensus and its explanation is that linguistically it is the road on which one walks. Since its literal meaning is impossible here, it must be taken metaphorically. It is either the statement of the people of consensus or the proof for which they reached consensus. The second is more appropriate due to the weaker connection between it and 'road' – that each one leads to the goal. The answer: 'the way' is also applied to consensus because linguists apply it to what a person chooses for himself from sayings or actions, including His statement: 'Say: This is my way.' If so, then applying it to consensus is more appropriate due to the generality of its benefit, for the two ways of consensus are used by both the mujtahid and the muqallid, while the proof is only used by the mujtahid.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and its answer in a dialectical exchange about the meaning of 'sabil' in the verse. The objector argues that the word 'sabil' (way) in 'Follow the way of the believers' cannot literally mean 'road', so it must be metaphorical. He says the metaphor could refer to either the consensus itself or the evidence (dalil) that led to consensus, and the latter is preferable because the connection between 'evidence' and 'road' (both lead to the goal) is weaker? Actually he says the second is more appropriate (أولى) due to the weaker relationship (قلة العلاقة). This is counterintuitive; perhaps he means the evidential proof has a weaker relationship to 'road' than the consensus statement, so it is more metaphorical? The answer refutes this by arguing that 'sabil' also applies to consensus because linguists use it for any chosen path of action or speech, citing the Quranic verse. The answerer claims that applying to consensus is better because of its broader benefit – both mujtahid and layperson can follow consensus, whereas only a mujtahid can follow the proof. Thus the answer defends the standard view that 'sabil' refers to consensus.

Key Terms
سبيل (sabil): In this passage, 'sabil' is debated whether it means consensus (ijma') or the proof (dalil) leading to consensus. The answerer argues it can mean both but consensus is more appropriate. [Note: No significant shift; standard usage in usul al-fiqh.]
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of scholars; here it is the object of following, and the objector disputes that it is meant literally. [Note: Standard term in usul al-fiqh, no shift.]

B) Because consensus is more frequently used in language.
C) Because the proof is only used by the mujtahid.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
The answer defeats the objection by providing linguistic evidence (Quranic usage) and a teleological argument (general benefit) to defend 'sabil' as consensus.

Repetition Refutes Restriction
Objection that following the believers is only required in matters pertaining to faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse a dialectical objection and identify the key move in the response that defends textual generality.

Core Idea
Repetition in divine speech is a flaw; interpretation must avoid it.

In this lesson, we examine a typical jadal (dialectical) exchange. The objector attempts to restrict the meaning of a Qur'anic command by appealing to the occasion of revelation. The answerer refutes this by showing that such restriction leads to a rhetorical defect. This illustrates how Islamic dialecticians defend the coherence and eloquence of the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
State precisely what the objector is challenging: that following the believers is obligatory only in matters of faith, not in all things.
The objector uses the occasion of revelation (an apostate) to limit the verse's scope.
2
Locate the objector's evidence
The objector cites that the verse was revealed concerning a man who apostatized, implying it specifically addresses apostasy.
This is a common move: use asbab al-nuzul to restrict a general command.
3
Identify the answerer's refutation strategy
The answerer argues that such an interpretation would make 'following the way of the believers' identical to 'not opposing the Messenger', creating unnecessary repetition.
The answerer shows that the restriction introduces a rhetorical flaw (takrar).
4
Explain why the refutation succeeds
Repetition in divine speech is considered defective in balagha, so the verse must have a broader meaning. Thus, the objection fails.
This demonstrates how eloquence serves as a criterion for interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : لا نسلم أنه يجب اتباع سبيل المؤمنين في كل شيء، بل في السبيل الذي صاروا به مؤمنين ويدل عليه أن الآية الكريمة نزلت في رجل ارتد، والجواب : أن هذا الاعتراض يلزم منه أن تكون مخالفة سبيل المؤمنين هي المشاقة، لأنه لا معنى لمشاقة الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام إلا ترك الإيمان، فلو حمل على هذا لزم التكرار والتكرار مما يخالف البلاغة، ولا يكون من الله عز وجل .
The fifth: We do not accept that following the way of the believers is obligatory in everything, but only in the way by which they became believers. This is supported by the fact that the noble verse was revealed concerning a man who apostatized. The answer: This objection necessarily implies that opposing the way of the believers is opposing the Messenger, because opposing the Messenger has no meaning other than abandoning faith. If it is interpreted this way, then it would be repetition, and repetition contradicts eloquence, and it cannot be from Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and its answer. The objector argues that the obligation to follow the believers is limited to matters of faith (the way by which they became believers), citing that the verse was revealed about an apostate. The answerer refutes this by showing that such an interpretation would make the phrase 'following the way of the believers' redundant, since it would simply mean 'following the way of faith', which is already implied by 'opposing the Messenger' in the same verse. Repetition is rhetorically defective, so the verse must mean something broader. Thus the answer defends the general obligation to follow the believers in all matters.

Key Terms
مشاقة
تكرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because it contradicts the literal meaning of 'sabil'.
D) Because it ignores the consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
An objection restricting a general command to its occasion of revelation fails if it would introduce unnecessary repetition in the divine text.

Refuting the Illusion of Neutrality in Jadal
Refutation of a false neutral third option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute a false neutral third option in a jadal exchange using the binary collapse strategy.

Core Idea
Neutrality is impossible: not following is still following the other side.

In jadal, an objector often tries to carve out a middle ground. This passage shows how the answerer destroys that ground by proving that the proposed neutral position actually falls under the prohibited category. This is a powerful dialectical maneuver used across Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's proposed third option
The objector grants that following non-believers is prohibited, but argues that one does not have to follow believers either. The third option is simply not following at all—neither believers nor non-believers.
This is an attempt to escape the binary.
2
Locate the answerer's refutation
The answerer states: 'Abandoning following entirely is part of following other than the way of the believers.' In other words, non-following is classified as following the non-believers.
The key move: reclassifying the neutral act into the prohibited category.
3
Explain why the third option collapses
The only alternatives are following believers or not following believers. 'Not following believers' is equivalent to 'following non-believers' because any deviation from their way is following something else. Thus the binary is exhaustive and the third option disappears.
The answerer uses a logical dichotomy: either you follow A or you don't. If you don't, you are following non-A.
4
Generalize the strategy
In any jadal, when an objector proposes a seemingly neutral option, check whether that option can be shown to fall under one of the existing opposing categories. If so, the neutral is false and the opponent's position is strengthened.
This strategy is common in usul al-fiqh and kalam debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : مع التسليم بتحريم اتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين، لكن لا نسلم وجوب اتباع سبيلهم، لأن هناك اختيارًا وسطا، وهو ترك الاتباع أصلا فلا يتبع سبيل المؤمنين ولا غير سبيلهم، والجواب أن ترك الاتباع بالكلية، هو من اتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين فمن اختاره لنفسه فقد اتبع غير سبيلهم .
The sixth: Granting the prohibition of following other than the way of the believers, but we do not accept the obligation to follow their way, because there is a middle option: not following at all, so one follows neither the way of the believers nor other than their way. The answer: Abandoning following entirely is part of following other than the way of the believers. Whoever chooses it for himself has indeed followed other than their way.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection and its answer. The objector proposes a third option: neither following the believers nor opposing them, but simply not following at all. The answerer refutes this by arguing that 'not following' is itself a kind of following of the non-believers, because the only alternative to following the believers is following something else, even if that 'something else' is passive non-engagement. Thus the dichotomy is exhaustive: one either follows the believers or follows something else, and the latter is prohibited. So following the believers is obligatory.

Key Terms
اتباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the answer to the sixth objection refute the possibility of a neutral position?
A) By proving that neutrality is logically impossible.
B) By arguing that complete abandonment of following is itself following the non-believers.
C) By citing a Quranic verse that prohibits neutrality.
D) By stating that consensus requires active following.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a proposed neutral option must be examined: if it can be reclassified under the opponent's prohibited category, the dichotomy is closed and the objection fails.

?
2. Identify another jadal exchange from Islamic history (e.g., in kalam on the attributes of Allah) where a neutral third option was proposed and refuted. Describe the binary collapse.

Seventh Objection: Following Not Required in Mubah
Objection that following is not required in permissible actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the logical structure of a jadal objection and its answer regarding following consensus on permissible acts.

Core Idea
Following the consensus on mubah means believing in its permissibility, not performing it as wajib.

This lesson dissects the seventh objection against requiring following in all matters. The objector claims that if consensus on a mubah obliged following, the act would become wajib, which is absurd. The answerer reframes the obligation as one of belief and intention, preserving the analogy with following the Prophet. You will learn to identify the roles of claimant and objector in a jadal exchange and assess the validity of the response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Find the objector's assertion: that following is not required in all matters because if consensus on a mubah made following obligatory, the mubah would become wajib, which is a contradiction.
The objector uses a reductio ad absurdum: if following were required in all, then mubah would become wajib, but mubah and wajib are distinct.
2
Extract the answerer's counter
The answerer responds by comparing following the believers to following the Prophet: following in mubah is obligatory, but the obligation is not to perform the act as wajib, but rather to believe it is permissible and do it with that intention.
The key move is distinguishing the type of obligation: it's an obligation of belief and intention, not of performance.
3
Analyze the analogy
The answerer draws an analogy: just as following the Prophet in mubah is obligatory but the Prophet's actions are not all emulated as wajib, so too is following the consensus. The exclusion of mubah from general imitation did not undermine the evidence for following the Prophet.
This shows that the objector's reasoning is based on a misunderstanding of what 'obligation to follow' entails.
4
Determine the outcome
By the rules of jadal, the answerer successfully defends the claim that following consensus is required in all matters, including mubah, by clarifying the meaning of obligation.
The objector's reductio fails because it misconstrues the nature of the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : مع التسليم بوجوب الاتباع، إلا أنه لا يجب في كل الأمور، لأنهم لو أجمعوا على فعل مباح لا يجب متابعتهم على فعله وإلا لكان المباح واجبا، وإذا لم يجب اتباعهم في الكل لم يلزم اتباعهم فيما أجمعوا عليه لجواز أن يكون المراد هو الإيمان أو غيره مما هو متفق عليه والجواب : أن اتباع سبيل المؤمنين كاتباع الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم فاتباعهم في المباح أيضًا واجب، ومعنى وجوبه: وجوب اعتقاد إباحته وأن يفعله الفاعل على جهة الإباحة لا على جهة أخرى .. ثم إن المباح قد أخرج من عموم التأسي بالنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ولم يقدح في الدلالة على وجوب اتباعه، فكذلك الحال في اتباع سبيل المؤمنين .
The seventh: Granting the obligation of following, but it is not obligatory in all matters, because if they reach consensus on a permissible act, following them in doing it is not obligatory, otherwise the permissible would become obligatory. Since following them in all is not necessary, following them in what they have consensus on is not necessary, because it is possible that what is intended is faith or other agreed-upon matters. The answer: Following the way of the believers is like following the Messenger, so following them in permissible acts is also obligatory, and the meaning of its obligation is the obligation to believe in its permissibility and that the doer should do it with the intention of permissibility, not with another intention. Then the permissible has been excluded from the general principle of imitating the Prophet, and that did not undermine the evidence for the obligation to follow him, so it is the same regarding following the way of the believers.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection and its answer. The objector argues that if consensus on a permissible (mubah) act made it obligatory to follow, then the permissible would become obligatory, which is contradictory. Therefore, following is not required in all matters. The answerer responds by drawing an analogy with following the Prophet: following him in permissible acts is also obligatory, but the obligation is not to perform the act as wajib, but rather to believe it is permissible and to do it with that intention. Just as the Prophet's actions are a source of emulation, so are the actions of the consensus. The exclusion of permissible acts from the obligation of imitation in the case of the Prophet did not nullify the general principle, so the same applies to the believers' consensus.

Key Terms
مباح
تأسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the seventh objection, what does the obligation to follow the believers in a permissible act mean?
A) One must perform the act as obligatory.
B) One must believe the act is permissible and do it with that intention.
C) One must avoid the act to prevent confusion.
D) One must follow only if the act is recommended.

Key Takeaway
Following consensus on mubah does not turn the act into wajib; it obligates belief in its permissibility and intention accordingly, preserving the integrity of ijma'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The objector (mu'tarid) argues that if following were obligatory, the act would become wajib, which is contradictory. Formulate an answer (jawab) using the same strategy as the passage: distinguish between the obligation to believe it is recommended and to perform it with that intention.
